In addition, certain tax entities for which data on imports/exports between the Czech Republic and the Slovak Republic were found in both databases were selected for the verification of the transactions reported on VAT returns.
4.3. Pilot check at Starý Hrozenkov-Drietoma border customs crossing
The border customs crossing for the performance of the pilot inspection was selected on the basis of a joint analysis of unassigned JCD items in the customs databases of the CR and SR.
Pursuant to a bilateral agreement between the Customs Authorities of the Czech Republic and the Slovak Republic, the use of control sheets has been introduced at border customs crossing points between the Czech Republic and the Slovak Republic since 1 June 1998.
The aim of this measure was to eliminate customs and tax evasion as far as possible, to provide an overview of the movement of means of transport transporting goods of a commercial nature within the customs area and to create the conditions for comparing customs registers with each other.
The joint pilot inspection was carried out from 8.3.2004 to 26.3.2004 and aimed at examining this system of customs control.
Procedure for the use of control sheets at the Starý Hrozenkov-Drietoma border customs crossing:
1. Considering the vehicle, issuing a checklist and handing it to the driver.
2) Unloading the vehicle in the designated sector in the parking lot.
Customs discussions in the check-in hall of the Policia ČR, Policie SR, Customs Administration of the Czech Republic, Customs Administration of the Slovak Republic.
The check-in process was confirmed by each component stamping the control sheet.
3) Bringing the vehicle into the physical inspection area.
Presentation of the control sheet, JCD, or other documents, as appropriate, to the customs officer - check that all prescribed acts and documents have been carried out.
Execution of physical checks by relevant files.
Releasing the vehicle from customs.
Posru: When importing goods into the Czech Republic, the weighing vehicles were carried out directly by the Customs Administration of the Czech Republic.
When the goods were imported into SR, the vehicles weighed were carried out by the staff of the authorised legal entity.
The vehicles were then entered into a computerised record.

Exports from the Czech Republic to the Slovak Republic

4.3.2. Control sheets and their records Exports from the Czech Republic to the Slovak Republic
When exported from the Czech Republic to the Slovak Republic, the certificates of inspection were issued and registered by the Customs Administration of the Slovak Republic.
The Customs Administration of the Czech Republic recorded the registration numbers of these control sheets in an electronic database of exports.
Out of 453 JCD entries, the SAO SR tracked 256 cases of imports into the Slovak Republic, according to data from the Customs database of the Czech Republic and the Slovak Republic. The remaining 197 JCD items were identified by the SAO of the Slovak Republic in cooperation with the workers of the Drietoma border customs crossing.
Exports from SR to CR

Out of a total of 339 JCD entries of the selected control sample, the SAO in cooperation with border customs crossing staff, Starý Hrozenkov assigned to 295 JCD entries the corresponding registration numbers of the control sheets.
The other 44 cases were identified in their records by the border customs checkpoint staff at Drietoma and the export of goods from SR to the Czech Republic was confirmed.
The SAO and SAO SR audit did not find deficiencies in the registration of selected cases of imports and exports of goods in 2002 at the Old Hrozenkov-Drietoma border customs crossing.
4.4. Selected control findings when verifying business transactions
In the framework of international cooperation, the realisation of imports and exports between the Czech Republic and the Slovak Republic was verified from the supporting documents of customs administrations and financial and tax offices in the territory of the second state.
The following were verified for selected tax entities:
- whether the submitted JCD were registered in the customs databases of the Czech Republic and the Slovak Republic,
- the fulfilment of the legal conditions for the application of the deduction of VAT on importation, the refund of excess VAT and the application of the exemption from VAT on exportation.
The verification of the commercial transactions found:
1. The SAO of SR requested verification of the implementation of alcohol exports at a statistical value of 56 989 Sk.
The SAO identified the corresponding imports in the Czech Customs Database at a statistical value of 42,624 CZK. r
2. The photographic equipment and accessories import and export company claimed an excess VAT deduction of CZK 3,740,664 in its VAT return for the month of December 2002.
In order to check reported exports from the Czech Republic, the SAO selected 3 JCD (16 items) with a statistical value of 1,274,822 CZK.
The SAO of SR identified 3 corresponding import JCD (17 items) at a statistical value of 1,724,321 Sk.
VAT was paid in the SR to the competent customs office.
3. The company, whose main business was the optical equipment business, applied a total excess VAT deduction in 2002 that was 4,1,36,000 CZK higher than the tax liability.
To verify the reported business activity, the SAO selected the export JCD (32 items) at a statistical value of 7,154,124 CZK.
The SAO SR found that this JCD corresponds to an import JCD (12 items) with a statistical value of 9,653,827 Sk.
VAT was paid in the SR to the competent customs office.
4. The company involved in, among other things, the purchase of goods for resale and sale, did not report any exports of goods in the 2002 and 2003 VAT returns filed.
According to the Customs database of the Czech Republic, the statistical value of goods exports totalled 3,439,888 CZK in 2002.
Based on these facts, the SAO selected for verification 5 export JCD (5 items) for a total statistical value of CZK 669,839.
The SAO of SR identified 5 corresponding import JCD (5 items) at a statistical value of 1 012 656 Sk.
The audit of the SAO of the Slovak Republic at the competent tax office showed that the declared imports were carried out and VAT paid.
5. The company, whose business included the purchase of goods for resale, manufacture, installation and repair of electrical machinery and apparatus, declared exports of goods worth CZK 12,966,706 in VAT returns in 2002.
According to the Customs database of the Czech Republic, in 2002 the total value of exports was 14,474,381 CZK.
The SAO submitted 17 export JCDs (59 items) for a total statistical value of 2,975,122 CZK.
The SAO SR identified 13 JCD (50 items) in the SR Customs Database, with a statistical value of 3 621 370 Sk.
The SR Customs Database failed to identify the corresponding import JCD to 4 export JCD (9 items) totalling a statistical value of 399,776 CZK.
The relevant tax office in SR verified these imports with the taxpayer.
The tax entity, listed on the JCD as an importer into SR, commented in the minutes of the local investigation that it had not carried out the imports declared on the Czech export JCD.
The SAO of SR therefore asked the local competent tax office to carry out a VAT and corporation tax audit for the tax years 2002 and 2003.
6. A company involved in the import and sale of cigarettes exported tobacco stickers worth CZK 7,338,199 in 2002 and CZK 12,228,545 in 2003, according to the Customs Database of the Czech Republic.
In its VAT returns, the company reported no exports in 2002 and 2003.
The SAO selected for verification 5 export JCD (5 items) totalling a statistical value of 2,471,598 CZK.
For 4 export JCD (4 items) in a total statistical value of 2,246,860 CZK, the SAO SR identified 7 JCD (7 items) in a statistical value of 2,919,514 Sk in the Customs Database SR.
A corresponding import could not be attributed to the export JCD (1 item) in statistical value of CZK 224,738 in the SR customs database.
7. The software delivery company did not report goods exports in its 2002 and 2003 VAT returns.
According to the Customs database of the Czech Republic, the statistical value of goods exports in 2002 and 2003 totalled 1,630,198 CZK.
The SAO selected 9 JCD (9 items) for verification in SR, with a total statistical value of CZK 755,588.
In the Customs Database SR, the SAO identified 8 JCD (8 items) in statistical value 915 601 Sk.
The export JCD (1 item) with a statistical value of £68,286 could not be attributed to the corresponding import in the SR customs database.
In those cases, it was not possible to identify in the SR customs database the corresponding imports to 6 JCD with a statistical value of 692,800 CZK.
Of which:
- 2 export JCD from the CR Customs Database at a statistical value of 293,024 CZK, the SR Customs Database failed to identify the corresponding import JCD (see paragraphs 6 and 7).
Due to a certain error rate in the entry of data into the customs database, it was not possible to identify some of the registered imports, e.g. in cases where the name of the importer was missing or there was an error in the tax identification number (TIN).
It can therefore not be established with certainty that those exports did not take place.
- 4 export JCDs from the Customs database of the Czech Republic were not shown to have imports corresponding to exports from the Czech Republic to the Slovak Republic at a statistical value of 399,776 CZK (see point 5).
In such cases, there is a reasonable suspicion of the fictitious export of the goods for the purpose of improperly claiming the VAT deduction.
5. Control of imports and exports of goods and services within the control of selected financial and tax authorities
5.1. Check pattern
In order to verify the fulfilment of the obligations of taxable persons in respect of imports and exports of goods and services and to evaluate the level of cooperation between financial and customs authorities, the files of taxable persons have been selected at the audited financial and tax offices, according to the following criteria:
1. tax subjects were selected from the customs databases of the Czech Republic and the Slovak Republic: (a) with the highest volume of imports, (b) with the highest amount of VAT charged, (c) with the highest volume of exports,
(a) the VAT deductions applied on importation; (b) the excess VAT deductions applied; (c) the right to a refund of SpD; (d) the VAT exemption applied on exportation;
3. tax entities that have been subject to a tax audit.
The sampling was carried out irrespective of the country of destination with a focus on risky commodities.
5.2. Checking findings in the Czech Republic
An overview of imports into the Czech Republic and exports from the Czech Republic for risky commodities in 2002 and 2003 is given in the table below:
5.2.1. Inspection of selected sample at tax offices
The SAO focused on the audited financial authorities in particular on:
- the process of the tax authorities in verifying the accuracy of the VAT declared,
- the cooperation of the tax authorities with the customs administration,
- Comparing data from the customs database with the JCD database at the tax offices with a focus on comparing the declared value, applying the correct VAT rate following the tariff classification of goods or services, or comparing data on JCD and accompanying invoices with data in the customs database,
- Comparing data from VAT returns on transactions made and received from abroad with data in the JCD database at tax offices or in the customs database,
- how to verify entitlement to VAT exemption when exporting goods or services abroad;
- a comparison of the volume of exports of goods or services declared in VAT returns with data from the customs database.
The JCD database at the tax offices contained only records of those imports and exports in respect of which the taxpayer was obliged to enter the import or export on a tax return.
At the audited tax offices, the files of the tax entities were examined, which reported imports totalling CZK 60,396,379,000 in 2002 and 2003 in the tax returns, with corresponding VAT totalling CZK 12,644,052,000.
Furthermore, the files of tax entities reporting exports worth a total of CZK 47,323,111 in tax returns for 2002 and 2003 were checked.
According to the Customs Database of the Czech Republic in 2002 and 2003, the tax subjects who were examined for imports and exports between the Czech Republic and the Slovak Republic carried out imports into the Czech Republic from the Slovak Republic with a total VAT base of CZK 3,759,352,000, VAT of CZK 767,607,000 and exports from the Czech Republic to the Slovak Republic with a total statistical value of CZK 1,989,090,000.
The detected differences between the records in the JCD database at the tax offices and in the Customs database of the Czech Republic were examined as follows:
- JCD items that were missing from the CR Customs Database were requested directly from the General Directorate of Customs of the CR,
- the JCD items that were missing from the tax authorities' database were verified as not being cancelled documents,
- for JCD items where different values were found between the Customs database of the Czech Republic and the Financial Offices database, the relevant JCD was requested from the General Directorate of Customs of the Czech Republic in written form including the accompanying invoices.
The examination revealed that the JCD database at the tax offices did not contain, for example, records of import JCDs with a total value of VAT of 3,622,000 CZK and records of export JCDs with a total statistical value of 9,359,000 CZK.
5.2.1.1. Differences found
A comparison of the JCD database at the tax offices and the accumulated tax liability for 2002 and 2003 in the VAT returns for a selected sample of tax subjects revealed the following differences:
The differences in values were mainly due to:
- The time lag between the realisation of the imports and the exercise of the right to deduct the VAT charged on the importation of the goods.
The taxpayer could exercise the right to deduct on importation of the goods within three years of the end of the tax year in which the goods were placed under the relevant scheme or, in the case of the temporary importation scheme, within three years of the end of the tax year in which the scheme came to an end.
- The time lag between the export performance and the time when the taxpayer received the JCD.
The taxable person was required to enter the details of the exports effected, exempt from the obligation to apply output tax, in the VAT return at the earliest for the tax year in which the goods left the country, if he also had the tax document that was JCD.
- Failure to comply with the obligations of taxable persons when reporting imports and exports on VAT returns.
In some cases, the taxpayer did not enter the correct import and export details in the VAT returns.
Since tax entities did not have to attach JCD lists to filed VAT returns, it was made more difficult for tax authorities to control the right to deduct VAT or VAT exemption.
5.2.2. Synergies between financial and customs authorities
During the period under scrutiny, import or export duties and VAT were repaid or remitted as a result of decisions taken by the customs authorities.
The taxpayer was required by law to reduce the previously claimed right to deduct VAT by the amount of tax refunded by the customs office, in the tax return for the period in which the tax was refunded.
No legislation provided for the sending of information on changes in the amount of duty and VAT from customs offices to tax offices.
Some audited tax authorities received this information, but irregularly or with significant delays, and only from some customs offices.
One audited IRS did not receive this information at all.
If the tax office did not receive information on changes in the amount of duty and VAT directly from the customs office, it did not have the data needed to check that the legal obligation on tax subjects was fulfilled.
In 2002, customs refunded VAT of more than CZK 319 million and in 2003 of more than CZK 398 million.
The results of the inspection pointed to persistent weaknesses in the activities of the tax authorities, some systemic errors in the cooperation of the tax authorities with the customs authorities and the failure of some of the obligations of the payers filing VAT returns and SpD.
Shortcomings in VAT and SpD management were identified in particular in the following areas:
(a) on the cancellation of VAT registrations, the taxable person's obligation to pay VAT on the business property registered or to be registered on the date of the cancellation of the registration in respect of which the taxable person has deducted VAT has been verified only in isolated cases;
(b) the tax authorities have not consistently exercised the option to carry out a tax audit or a local investigation.
This has in some cases led to inadequate verification of the legitimacy of the right to deduct VAT,
(c) official records and minutes of oral proceedings did not contain all the necessary particulars proving that the excess VAT claimed was justified;
(d) the tax authorities did not sufficiently verify the imports and exports declared in the VAT returns.
5.3. SR control findings
5.3.1. Inspection of selected sample at tax offices
For the selected sample, the SAO audited or the tax office carried out a tax audit for the tax years 2002 and 2003.
After analysing the submitted tax returns and refund claims with the selected tax entities, the SAO of SR requested the tax office to secure the relevant JCDs showing import and export, invoices and proof of payment of tax.
Subsequently, the SAO SR checked whether the submitted JCD was registered in the customs database of imports and exports and the fulfilment of the decisive facts and legal conditions in the application of the deduction of input tax on imports, in the refund of excess VAT, in the application of the exemption from export tax and in the refund of SpD.
The SAO SR at 10 audited tax offices verified selected cases of imports and exports of goods between the SAR and the Czech Republic in the structure according to the number of JCD items and the sum of statistical values shown in the table below.
In total, imports were checked at a statistical value of 856.2 M. Sk, representing 5.11% of the total value of imports of selected commodities from the Czech Republic to the SROV.
Exports of selected commodities were checked for a total statistical value of 176 million units, representing 6.87% of the total value of exports of selected commodities from the SR to the Czech Republic.
In addition, 618 cases of imports of other goods and services (mainly white tech, packaging and software) from EU states to SR were checked for a total statistical value of 532.8 M. Sk.
5.3.2. Synergies between tax and customs
The SAO SR's audit of tax offices found discrepancies between the data registered in the Customs Database of the Slovak Republic and the data reported in specific VAT returns, e.g.:
- 2 tax subjects (natural persons) declared in their tax returns imports of goods totalling 1 589 261 Sk, on the basis of which they claimed a refund of excess VAT deductions.
In local investigations carried out at the instigation of the SAO of the Slovak Republic, tax subjects failed to prove to the Tax Office the validity of the application of input tax by the relevant documents of the realisation of the imports and the payment of VAT,
- the tax entity (s.r.o.) declared in its tax return for the January 2003 tax year the taxable transaction received in the value of 77 167 576 Sk, the export of goods in the value of 18 006 041 Sk, and claimed an excess VAT deduction of 17 157 101 Sk.
The VAT returns filed for 2003 show that the difference between the volume of purchases and exports of 59 161 535 Sk, which arose in January 2003, was not compensated during the whole of 2003.
In the above cases, the SAO requested the competent tax authorities to carry out a tax audit of the excess VAT deductions applied.
- in another case, the tax entity (s.r.o.) for the tax year January to June 2002 declared exports of goods worth a total of 87,199 Sk in VAT returns.
A verification in the SR customs database revealed that the taxpayer had exported a statistical value of 296 747 Sk. during that period.
The Tax Office carried out multiple local investigations at the company's headquarters, finding that the company's manager (a UK citizen) had travelled and the company had left the rented premises without terminating the lease in August 2002.
At the time of the audit of the SAO, the tax administration filed a report to the law enforcement authorities on the suspicion of a crime of non-payment of tax.
Based on the results of the audit at selected tax offices, the SAO finds that the biggest problems in the administration and control of VAT and SpD were mainly in the following areas:
- non-use of the option to carry out a VAT tax check on problematic tax entities, in particular in cases where a high value of excess VAT is applied;
- Tax control protocols did not include an inventory of the documents checked
- JCD on the import and export of goods,
- Insufficient verification of declared imports and exports of goods in tax returns;
- the lengthy procedure for tax administrations to make decisions, in particular in the context of the assessment procedure, even with the consequence of forfeiting the right to impose penalties;
- Failure to comply with legal conditions when granting tax deferrals;
- non-recovery of VAT arrears within the VAT excess refund deadline;
- underutilisation of all forms of security and recovery of tax arrears.
Based on the audit of the observed shortcomings, the SAO of the Slovak Republic asked the competent tax authorities to carry out a VAT tax audit focusing on the legitimacy of the claimed excess VAT deductions of 14 tax entities.
Measures have been taken at all audited tax offices to remedy the weaknesses identified, the implementation of which will be monitored and checked by the SAO.
6. Conclusion
International cooperation has made it possible to examine trade transactions made between trading partners from both countries and has highlighted deficiencies in the record of imports and exports.
Furthermore, the inspections resulted in the detection of persistent deficiencies in the compliance of VAT payers and SpD, in the activity of the financial and tax authorities respectively, in the system of cooperation and exchange of information between tax and customs administrations, which ultimately reduced the efficiency and effectiveness of tax controls.
The lessons learned also showed that the tax authorities in the Czech Republic have basic data on imports and exports from the customs database of the Czech Republic under the tax information system. In the SR, there is no similar system for the transmission of information on imports and exports of goods between customs and tax administrations.
The procedure for verifying the performance of the VAT administration and SpD was not the same, given the different legislation and conditions in the territory of the two States.
The SAO verified the necessary data from information obtained with the Czech Customs Administration and from documents available at audited tax offices. The SAO of the Slovak Republic verified the execution of commercial transactions from documents of tax entities requested through the tax authorities.
If irregularities were found, he asked the tax authorities to carry out tax checks.
Parallel VAT and SpD controls carried out assessed the state of administration of these taxes in the run-up to accession to the European Union and pointed to the need for further cooperation in this area.
Mutual cooperation will be further enhanced in monitoring the activities of financial and tax authorities in the context of the adoption of the new VAT law.
One of the new obligations related to the delivery of goods to other Member States of the European Union is the submission of a so-called recapitulative statement.
This report must be made by all VAT payers who have made a supply of goods to another Member State to a person registered for VAT in another Member State.
Tax administrators are required to enter the data from the recapitulative statements into the VIES (VAT Information Exchange System) system, to which tax administrators from the Member States of the European Union have access.
The financial or tax authorities then have the possibility to compare this data with the data contained in the submitted VAT returns.
This system is further used to verify the validity of VAT registration number of persons, verify the validity of the VAT exemption applied on the supply of goods to another Member State and check the proper taxation of the goods.
The implementation of parallel control actions showed the possibilities of cooperation between the SAO and the SAO of the Slovak Republic.
The results of these control actions will confront the outputs of the control actions being prepared, which will inter alia focus on examining the VIES system to prevent tax evasion in so-called 'intra-Community supplies'.
Given the importance of VAT and SpD for budgetary revenue, the need to focus control activities primarily on monitoring and identifying risk areas in order to reduce the scope for tax evasion on indirect taxes.
Communiqué
This report was prepared in Czech, English and Slovak languages and signed by the Vice-President of the Supreme Audit Office and the President of the Najvyššie Audit Office of the Slovak Republic.
Report on results of asylum checks in the Czech Republic and the Slovak Republic
State assets and funds intended to safeguard the activities of the Ministry of the Interior's Refugee Facilities Administration
Control action No 04/23 "State property and funds intended to safeguard the activities of the Ministry of the Interior's Administration of Refugee Facilities"
Control action No 15/2004 ‘Control of hospodárenia with staff assets and funds intended to provide asylum and temporary sanctuary cudzincom in SR’
1. Introduction
The migration of the population is one of the phenomena that are global in nature and have become an integral part of the current development phase of the Czech Republic and the Slovak Republic.
It is mainly due to the flight of citizens from their country of origin or last place of residence from persecution, in particular for reasons of race, religion, nationality, politics, as well as the consequences of military conflicts and ethnic cleansing.
Their aim is to gain asylum and integration into society, especially in some of the advanced democratic countries of Europe.
Foreigners are also a large group, driven by unfavourable economic and living conditions.
Migration is not a new phenomenon.
The adoption of the 1951 Geneva Agreement and the 1967 New York Protocol on the Status of Refugees, both of which the governments of the signatory countries have pledged to apply in their foreign-policy orientations, has already established specific principles for helping refugees.
The Czech and Slovak Federative Republic ratified the New York Protocol in 1991 and the Geneva Agreement in 1992.
Both the Czech Republic and the Slovak Republic declared their position on the refugee issue after the split in 1993 by continuing to implement adopted international documents, and their binding nature under national legislation was confirmed by a legal act.
Both republics have also enshrined the granting of asylum in their constitutional laws.
Parallel checks carried out by the Supreme Audit Office, the Czech Republic and the NajHigher Audit Office of the Slovak Republic were focused on the asylum process and related issues, the results of which are included in the joint final report.
Cooperation between the supreme audit institutions of the two countries was carried out on the basis of the "Control Cooperation Treaty" (the "Treaty") concluded in April 2004.
The two Parties have undertaken in the Treaty to provide each other with information on the legislation governing asylum procedures in the State of the other Party, selected statistics on the number of asylum seekers and the results of the asylum process, as well as information on asylum facilities.
The aim of the parallel control action was to identify the same problem areas and to highlight the consequences of this for the area of population migration.
Cooperation consisted in the unification of the programme of control actions, the exchange of information within the scope set out in the Treaty and the drawing up of a joint final report on the outcome of controls.
The first joint meeting, which took place from 5.4 to 7.4.2004, set out identical sets of controlled issues and control procedures in the preparation of control actions.
At the second meeting, which took place from 28.6 to 30.6.2004, the common points for the control action programme were specified.
During the third joint meeting, which took place during the audit event from 8.11 to 10.11.2004, there was an exchange of experience on ongoing inspections.
The modalities of the outputs from the controls have been specified and the structure and content of the joint final report, which was drawn up on 14.2 to 17.2.2005 at the last joint meeting, has been provisionally determined.
The checks were carried out in parallel between September 2004 and January 2005.
In the Czech Republic, inspectors of the Defence and Security Department and selected territorial unions carried out the inspection.
The audited persons were the Ministry of the Interior (MV) and the Administration of Refugee Facilities of the Ministry of the Interior (SUZ).
In the Slovak Republic, the section of defence, vnútra and štátnych úradov was inspected.
The audited entity was the Migračný úrad Ministerstva vnútra SR (Ministry of Interior).
The audited period was 2001, 2002, 2003 (the audited period).
In some cases, statistics for a longer period of time are also used in the joint report.
2. Review of control results
2.1. Assessment of control activities
The subject of the checks carried out in parallel were: - the asylum process, - the asylum facilities, - the management of the State's funds and assets, - the internal control system.
The mutual exchange of lessons learned between the Supreme Audit Office, the Czech Republic and the NajHigher Audit Office of the Slovak Republic took place at the stage of preparation and implementation of control actions.
This exchange allowed for a more thorough evaluation of the submissions, an analysis of the causes of the identified differences in the safeguarding of the asylum process.
This made it possible to focus on the critical issues affecting the operation of asylum facilities.
Ultimately, this had a positive effect on the quality of the control work carried out.
2.2 Summary of control results
The harmonisation of the asylum law of the Czech Republic and the Slovak Republic with European Union (EU) documents has been the subject of European Commission evaluation reports on the readiness of both countries to join the EU.
Legislation in this area continues after the accession of both countries to the EU with a view to further harmonisation with EU law.
The number of asylum seekers in the Czech Republic increased significantly in 2001.
In the Slovak Republic, the number of applicants is on a steadily growing trend, with a significant increase in applicants occurring in 2001.
The Czech Republic and the Slovak Republic, within the meaning of international agreements, provide financial and material security to asylum seekers.
Both countries' national budgets allocate the necessary funds for this purpose.
The amount of expenditure drawn from the national budget of the two countries is influenced in particular by the number of applicants, the duration of the asylum procedure until the granting or non-granting of asylum, as well as the state of health of the applicants.
The amount of some benefits collected is comparable in both countries.
The Czech Republic and the Slovak Republic established asylum facilities in which they gradually created suitable conditions for the stay of participants in asylum procedures.
Asylum facilities provide asylum seekers with accommodation, meals, health care and basic material equipment and hygiene supplies during their stay.
At the same time, courses are organised in leisure activities and the applicants are created the conditions for cultural and sporting enjoyment.
Asylum facilities include reception centres and residential centres, as well as integration centres in the Czech Republic.
The cost per stay day depends on the occupancy and amenities of the centre and is comparable in both countries.
In the Czech Republic, the capacity of asylum facilities is basically stabilised.
In the Slovak Republic, it is projected to increase by almost 1,000 seats in the next two years.
The aim is to create the conditions for applicants, an increasing number of whom are expected to continue to apply the Dublin Convention in the coming years.
In the Czech Republic, the highest part of the approved budget expenditure was spent on service security. The crucial expenditure under this heading was expenditure on accommodation and catering for applicants in asylum facilities operated under contract, expenditure on healthcare provided to asylum seekers and the cost of a permanent security service for the protection of asylum facilities.
Expenditure on salaries, payments for work carried out and insurance premiums also accounted for a significant proportion of total expenditure.
In the Slovak Republic, the budgetary management of the security of migration policy and the asylum process was marked by many budget measures addressing legitimate demands related to the increase in the number of asylum seekers.
Expenditure on wages, salaries and insurance premiums, expenditure on materials and services, and expenditure on security services have all contributed decisively to current spending.
The results of the audit in the Czech Republic and the Slovak Republic highlighted some similar deficiencies which did not have a major impact on the financial and asset management of the State.
These were particularly shortcomings in the use of State budget funds, contrary to the purpose for which they were determined by the Finance Act, when current expenditure covered actions having the character of a technical valuation of fixed tangible assets.
The Accounting Act on Inventories and on Non-Compliance with the Chart of Accounts was also breached.
In the Czech Republic, minor deficiencies were also found in the disposal of the state's property when it was left to be used by another person without a contract.
Failure to comply with the deadline for reporting changes to the Land Registry data breached the Land Registry Act and breached the Waste Act by leaving the waste for disposal to a person who was not the person authorised to handle the waste.
In addition, deficiencies were found in the Slovak Republic in the provision of unduly large advances for electricity and natural gas collection, deficiencies in public procurement and in the management of the state's assets when its record was not sufficiently secured and the method of disposing of unnecessary assets was not demonstrated.
The internal control system has been ensured in the Czech Republic and the Slovak Republic within the meaning of the general binding legislation in force governing the way financial control is carried out.
There were no deficiencies in the written documentation submitted.
Despite some shortcomings identified, the Supreme Audit Office, the Czech Republic and the Najvyšší Audit Office of the Slovak Republic are positive about the process of SUZ and the Ministry in securing services for asylum seekers.
Building on the lessons learned during the inspection, the Supreme Audit Office, the Czech Republic and the Najvyšší kontrolný úrad Slovenskej republiky note that, despite the significant increase in the number of asylum seekers in recent years, the respective organisational units of both countries have managed the tasks related to the care of asylum seekers at an appropriate level.
The evaluation shows that the system is functional and stable.
The results of the inspection were discussed with interested statutory representatives of the relevant organisational units of the state on both the Czech and Slovak sides.
Based on the results of the inspection, we recommend that the positive methodological experience of the inspection is used in other joint control actions within the international cooperation of supreme audit institutions.
3. Legislative framework
In both the Czech Republic and the Slovak Republic, asylum law is based on the 1951 Geneva Convention (the Convention relating to the Status of Refugees and the subsequent New York Protocol of 1967).
The first legislation was implemented by Act No 498/1990 Coll., on refugees.
In the Czech Republic, Act No 325/1999 Coll., on Asylum and Amending Act No 83/1991 Coll., on the Police of the Czech Republic, has been effective since 1 January 2000.
This Act has been amended a total of five times (Act No 2/2002 Coll., Act No 217/2002 Coll., Act No 320/2002 Coll., Act No 519/2002 Coll. and Act No 222/2003 Coll.).
There are currently two other amendments to Act No 325/1999 on Asylum in the legislative process, and the adoption of further amendments will be linked to European legislation.
At the time of law 325/1999 on asylum, the area of asylum was part of the 3rd pillar of the EU and documents on asylum were political documents in their binding nature.
Already, as part of the adoption of this law, there was an effort to make it as compatible as possible with EU law.
A major change for asylum policy within the EU was the transfer of asylum issues from the 3rd to 1st pillar, when asylum law became part of Community law from 1.5.1999 under the Treaty of Amsterdam.
This shift has created the need to create appropriate legislation as well, i.e. to create legal instruments in an appropriate binding legal order.
The compatibility of Czech asylum law with EU documents has been the subject of regular evaluation reports by the European Commission.
Key Community Asylum Regulations were adopted shortly before or after the Czech Republic's accession to the EU and their transposition period at the time of the Supreme Audit Office's inspection, the Czech Republic has not yet expired.
Once implemented in the Czech legal order, the Czech Republic will be obliged to notify the European Commission of how it has implemented the transposition.
In the Slovak Republic, the procedure of state authorities in the procedure for granting refugee status and determining the obligations of foreigners who have applied for refugee status, or who have been granted temporary refuge in the territory of the Slovak Republic, was laid down, with effect from 1 January 1996, by the National Council of the Slovak Republic Act No 283/1995 Z. z., on utechencoch.
Since 2003, the Refugee Act has been replaced by Act No 480/2002 Z. z ‘ on asylum and amending and supplementing certain laws, incorporating the harmonisation of the legislation of the Slovak Republic with Council of Europe Directive 2001/55/EC of 20.7.2001 on minimum standards for providing temporary protection in the event of a mass influx of displaced persons and on measures to promote the balance of efforts between Member States in receiving such persons and bearing the resulting consequences.
Following two amendments to Act No 480/2002 Z z., on asylum, in 2003 and 2004, a third amendment was approved at the beginning of 2005, which means further harmonisation of the law of the Slovak Republic with EU legislation on migration and asylum.
Its important elements include, for example, making it compulsory for asylum seekers to attend a Slovak language foundation course during their stay at the integration centre, appointing a guardian to the courts for non-legal aliens if they are staying in the territory of the Slovak Republic without a legal representative, allowing applicants to enter into an employment relationship in certain circumstances, as set out in an amendment to the law.
The harmonisation of the Slovak Republic's law with EU law in the area of migration and asylum process has been assessed in the European Commission's regular reports on the preparedness for the Slovak Republic's accession to the EU.
In 2002, the report pointed out that the Ministry's staffing capacity was inadequate, which did not correspond to the increased number of asylum seekers.
4. Asylum process
In the Czech Republic, matters relating to refugees have come under the jurisdiction of the MoI since 1990.
With effect from 1.1.1996, the MoI established an organisational arm of the state SUZ. This was due to the separation of the state administration's performance in the field of asylum procedure, which is carried out through the Department of Asylum and Migration Policy of the MoI, from the provision of services to asylum seekers.
The task of the SUZ is to provide accommodation, catering, health and other services for the MV to participants in asylum and asylum procedures.
In the Slovak Republic, the Ministry carries out crucial tasks in the field of asylum process, in particular in terms of financial and material-technical security of the asylum process, to the extent defined by specific regulations.
Priority tasks include issues related to deciding on asylum applications by aliens at stage I of the administrative procedure and the comprehensive operation of asylum facilities in the areas of accommodation, catering, health, financial and material security for asylum seekers.
It also prepares concepts for the state's migration policy.
The results achieved in the area of asylum system development in the Slovak Republic over the past years have been significantly influenced by cooperation and support from the Office of the United Nations High Commissioner for Refugees (UNHCR).
A particular benefit was his financial participation in the refurbishment of the Asylum Housing Fund.
UNHCR also co-financed social, cultural and sporting activities for applicants.
4.1 Developments in the number of asylum seekers and the results of the asylum process
The trend in the number of asylum seekers in the Czech Republic is shown in the graph.
There was a significant increase in 2001 due to the Kosovo crisis and in 2003 there was an increase in the number of refugees from Chechnya.
An overview of the results of the asylum procedure in the Czech Republic is given in Table 1.
A total of 394 cases were granted asylum in the period under scrutiny, including 83 in 2001, 103 in 2002 and 208 in 2003.
A total of 22,731 cases were not granted asylum during the period under review and 22,636 cases were discontinued.
The number of parties on 1.1 includes both those to whom the decision has not yet been issued and those to whom the decision has not been communicated.
Also included are those who have been excused from missing the deadline for lodging an appeal, or who have had a previous decision revoked and not yet issued a new one.
In the Slovak Republic, the number of asylum seekers is a steadily increasing trend.
If 96 applicants were registered in 1993, the figure was 10,358 in 2003.
There was a significant increase in the number of applicants in 2001, when 8,131 registered, an increase of 423% on 2000, when 1,556 applied for asylum.
Data on the results of asylum procedures in the Slovak Republic during the audited period are presented in Table 2.
A total of 49 asylum applications were granted during the period under scrutiny and the number did not exceed 0.22% of the registered number of applicants in any given year.
Asylum was not granted in 860 cases.
Asylum procedures were discontinued in a total of 23,088 cases.
The bulk of applicants, by staying away from the asylum facility without a permit, voluntarily left the asylum procedure before it was completed.
More than 82% of the total number of registered applicants in 2002 and more than 94% in 2003.
The length of an applicant's stay in an asylum facility, converted into an average number of days, was 13 days in 2001, 18 days in 2002 and 19 days in 2003.
The Ministry's findings also show that the majority of applicants entering the asylum process in the Slovak Republic are seeking better living conditions, i.e. so-called economic migration, which has no basis in the documents adopted by the international community, to which the Slovak Republic has also signed up.
In these cases, the asylum system is used for purpose by the applicants.
A request for asylum based on economic grounds, such as seeking employment or better living conditions, is not grounds for asylum.
Such a request should be assessed as manifestly unfounded under the provisions of Article 12(1)(a) of Act No 480/2002 on Asylum, and should be decided within 30 days.
There are still no internationally recognised principles for identifying economic migrants that distinguish them from applicants persecuted in their country of origin and that allow the asylum process to be accelerated.
In view of the above, it can be concluded that the Slovak Republic is a transit country in terms of international migration, and the destination countries of migration have been, and remain, Western European states.
After joining the EU, the Czech Republic and the Slovak Republic acceded to the Agreement determining the State responsible for examining asylum applications lodged in one of the Member States of the European Communities (Dublin Convention).
The Convention prevents multiple purpose-built asylum applications from being lodged in more than one EU country.
Applicants return to the country of first registration after a positive identification in EURODAC.
The Slovak Republic is part of the external border of EU countries, therefore the application of the Dublin Convention may in the next period result in an increase in the number of applicants who will need to create conditions to stay in the Slovak Republic until a decision is taken on whether or not to grant asylum.
4.2 Drawing on funds
The Czech Republic and the Slovak Republic are obliged to provide financial and material security to asylum seekers within the meaning of international agreements and resulting national legislative adaptations.
Both countries' national budgets allocate the necessary funds for this purpose.
The amount of expenditure drawn from the state budget is mainly influenced by the number of applicants, the duration of the asylum procedure up to the final decision on granting asylum, as well as their state of health.
The growing number of people entering the asylum process has a direct impact on the higher uptake of statutory benefits for claimants, such as food, pocket money, expenditure on basic material equipment, other material equipment and other social benefits.
Some doses selected are comparable in both countries.
In the Czech Republic, during the audited period, meals were 58 CZK per person per day, pocket money was 12 CZK per person per day and 6 CZK per child under 15 years of age.
The cost per asylum seeker per residence day was on average 351 CZK in 2001, 383 CZK in 2002 and 345 CZK in 2003.
In addition to the allowance, asylum seekers housed in residential centres with self-contained cooking and applicants housed in private out of asylum facilities may be granted a subsistence allowance up to the minimum.
Law 325/1999 Coll., on Asylum, as amended, provides, inter alia, in Paragraph 84, for municipalities to be entitled to a contribution towards the costs incurred in connection with an asylum facility on its territory.
The amount of the allowance was set each year by Government resolutions and during the audited period was CZK 7 per person per day of stay.
At the same time, municipalities are granted an allowance to cover the non-investment costs of primary schools attended by asylum seekers.
The amount of the allowance was £120 per pupil per month during the audited period, on condition that in that month the person attended the school for a minimum of 10 days.
In the Slovak Republic, in the controlled period, the diet was 70 Sk per person per day, the allowance was 12 Sk per person per day and 8 Sk per child under 15 years of age.
The cost per asylum seeker per residence day averaged 442 Sk in 2001, 447 Sk in 2002 and 445 Sk in 2003.
The municipalities in whose territory the asylum facility is located are granted a contribution towards the partial reimbursement of expenses incurred by the municipality in connection with the establishment and activities of the asylum facility, in accordance with Act No 480/2002 Z. z., on Asylum.
Municipalities have so far made only partial use of the possibility to apply for the allowance, which is set at 1 500 Sk per bed per calendar year.
National legislation in both countries sets a 90-day time limit for issuing asylum decisions from the start of asylum proceedings, while allowing for an extension of that time limit in justified cases.
The most common reason for extending the asylum procedure, which affects the level of state budget expenditure, is the lodging of appeals against decisions not to grant asylum.
In the Czech Republic, the highest number of final decisions were issued between 90 and 180 days (31.54%).
The second highest number was within 90 days of the date of the asylum application (17.61%).
In total, 73% of asylum procedures initiated between 2001 and 2003 were finally decided within one year of their start.
Within two years of the start of the asylum procedure, 92% of asylum applications were finally decided.
In the Slovak Republic, according to the Ministry's data, up to 90% of applicants who have not been granted asylum benefit from the statutory possibility of extending the deadline by appealing to the court.
In some cases it was an extension of more than a year and the court proceedings had not been completed at the time of the inspection.
Some benefit in shortening the asylum procedure is expected from the establishment of joint working places of the Ministry and the Department of Border and Cudzinecke Policie of the Police Board in reception centres in Adama-Gbeloch, Opatovskej Novej Vsi and in Liptovský Vlachoch, which was approached at the time of the inspection from 1.10.2004.
This envisages simplifying procedures and shortening procedural deadlines between the two arms of the asylum process.
5. Asylum facilities
The Czech Republic and the Slovak Republic established asylum facilities in which they gradually created suitable conditions for the provision of services to participants in the asylum procedure.
Asylum facilities provide asylum seekers with accommodation, meals, healthcare, pocket money and basic material equipment and hygiene supplies during their stay.
At the same time, courses are organised in leisure activities and the applicants are created the conditions for cultural and sporting activities.
5.1 Asylum facilities in the Czech Republic
SUZ manages and operates asylum facilities and is responsible for managing the state's assets in the areas of the reception centre of Vyšní Lhoty, the reception and residence centre of Red Újezd, the residential centres of Bělá-Jezová, Zastávka u Brno, Kostelec nad Orlicí, Havířov and the premises of the integration asylum centres of Jaroměř and Zastávka u Brno.
In early 2002, a reception centre was set up in the transit area of Prague-Ruzyně Airport.
SUZ also operates asylum facilities in Stráž pod Ralskem, Seči, Bruntále, Zbýšov and Kashava, which are operated by a legal entity on the basis of Article 80(3) of the Asylum Act No 325/1999 Coll.
The total accommodation capacity of reception centres is 747 places and the total accommodation capacity of residential centres is 1 977 places.
Reception centres are closed facilities which asylum seekers may not leave until they have completed the prescribed medical examinations and basic reception procedures specified in Act No 325/1999 on Asylum.
Violation of the obligation to stay at the reception centre is classed as a misdemeanour.
Accommodation centres to which asylum seekers come from reception centres are used to accommodate asylum seekers until a decision on their application to be granted or not granted asylum has become final.
Provided that certain conditions are met, asylum seekers are allowed to leave or stay in private.
Asylum seekers have the right to free accommodation, meals, other services and pocket money in the reception and reception centres in which they are reported to be staying, provided that they have tolerated the actions necessary to establish their financial and financial circumstances, and such ratios are such that even partial reimbursement for those services cannot be fairly claimed from them.
Integration asylum centres are used for the temporary accommodation of people who have been granted asylum.
Clients are housed in the centre under an accommodation contract, which is generally concluded for 6 months, with the possibility of extension until adequate housing can be provided for them, or secured by themselves under a state integration programme.
In the integration centre, clients pay all accommodation costs and eat individually.
Persons granted refugee status have the same rights and obligations as Czech citizens and have the same access to social security.
5.1.1 Cost of asylum facilities
To compare asylum facilities, it was chosen as the most meaningful indicator of the cost per physical day of residence.
It is based on the actual number of people who have actually been provided with the services provided by the asylum facility each day.
In Table 3 and in the graph, establishments operated by SUZs are designated as their own and those operated by legal persons under Section 80 of Act No 325/1999 Coll., on Asylum, as contracted.
The cost of one day's stay in its own SUZ-operated asylum facility is decreasing as these facilities become more populated. This is because the fixed costs of running the premises are allocated to more asylum seekers.
The total cost of providing services for asylum seekers increases proportionately as the number increases.
In 2001 and 2003, the asylum facilities themselves housed applicants in crisis capacity.
In part of 2003, extra accommodation capacity in residential containers (320 beds in total) was even used to deal with the capacity crisis in asylum facilities caused by the mass arrival of asylum seekers of Chechen nationality from Poland.
This capacity was provided through the lease of 80 residential and 6 sanitary containers.
Spending per applicant per residence day was then lower in those years.
However, long-term maintenance of high levels of fulfillment of these facilities is not possible.
The risks of an unexpected influx of refugees and the associated demand for the need for increased accommodation should be taken into account.
At the same time, it is not possible to carry out certain repairs, maintenance and investments with full accommodation capacity.
The form of asylum facilities operated contractually allows for the adoption of operational solutions on the capacity of asylum facilities where necessary.
5.2 Asylum facilities in the Slovak Republic
Asylum facilities are reception centres and residential centres.
The Ministry has in its administration the Adamov-Gbely reception centres, Rohovce, Opatovská Nová Ves and the Brezová residential centre under Bradlom.
The total capacity of the reception centres is 470 places and the residential centre 100 places.
A growing number of asylum seekers were dealt with by the Ministry by setting up a residential centre in Gabčíkov in the rented premises of the Slovak Technical University of Bratislava, with flexible available capacity ranging from 250 to 940 places.
Under the lease agreement, the leaseholder also provides services connected with the operation of the centre.
In this form, the MO addressed its own staff shortages.
The residential centre was also temporarily set up in 2002 and 2003 in rented premises in Jazierciach with a capacity of 160.
Reception centres averaged between 57% and 62% during the audited period; residential centres averaged between 33% and 50%.
Occupancy varied by season and was highest in the final months of the year, when many more asylum seekers applied compared to other months of the year.
Further increases of accommodation capacity of about 450 places will be possible after reconstruction of the centres in Opatovskej Novej Vsi and Vlachoch.
The number of places will increase by a further 550 people following the completion of the centre in Humennom in 2006.
5.2.1 Cost of asylum facilities
Figures for the cost of operating asylum facilities are given in Table 4.
Their analysis shows that the operation of an asylum facility by another entity providing premises and running facilities, including related activities, is one of the options to safeguard, at reasonable cost, the tasks arising from the asylum seekers' stay in the Slovak Republic.
6. Management of state funds and assets
6.1 Economic analysis
In the Czech Republic, the approved budgeted expenditure of SUZs was increased by 13 budget measures of CZK 103,077,000 in 2001.
Of this, 89 979,000 CZK (87.29%) were earmarked funds. The bulk was intended to deal with the consequences of the Kosovo crisis ($44,538,000) and to provide health care to asylum seekers ($38,870,000).
In 2002, SUZ expenditure was increased by 20 budget measures by CZK 88,362,000.
Of this, 62,282,000 CZK (70.48%) were dedicated funds, the bulk of which (53,200 CZK) was for health care provided to asylum seekers.
In 2003, SUZ expenditure was increased by 14 budget measures totalling CZK 88,078,000.
Of this, CZK 82,000,000 (93.09%) were earmarked funds released from the General Treasury Administration.
The amount of CZK 51,000,000 was for health care provided to asylum seekers, CZK 24,000,000 to deal with the influx of Chechen refugees, and CZK 7,000,000 for a humanitarian evacuation programme for disabled residents of the Republic of Iraq.
An overview of the execution of selected SUZ expenditure during the audited period is given in Table 5.
It is clear from the overview above that the highest proportion of expenditure was spent on service provision.
Spending on purchasing services ranged from 37.07% to 38.23% of total spending.
Excruciating expenses under this heading were expenses for housing and catering applicants in asylum facilities operated under contract, expenses for medical care provided to asylum seekers and the cost of a permanent security service to guard the various asylum facilities.
Salary, work-related payments and insurance premiums accounted for a significant proportion of SUZ's total expenditure (23.99% - 24.97%).
Last but not least, purchases of materials accounted for a significant share of SUZs' total expenditure (around 14%), with food purchases accounting for the highest proportion of such expenditure (7.61% ? 9.83% of total expenditure).
In the Slovak Republic, the Ministry's budget management of securing migration policy and the asylum process was marked by more budgetary measures that addressed legitimate demands linked to the increase in the number of asylum seekers.
The budget proposal and breakdown in the expenditure section was characterised, in particular in 2001 and 2002, by an imbalance between the IAC's requests, which were based on an increase in the number of asylum seekers and the financing needs of their legal entitlements, and the resources provided, above all, in the main category of current expenditure.
The budget chapter administrator tackled the disproportion during the year with multiple budget measures, increasing the current spending limit by 41.8% in 2001 and by as much as 71.7% in 2002.
An overview of the execution of selected expenses of the Ministry during the audited period is given in Table 6.
Expenditure on wages, salaries and insurance premiums, ranging from 24.47% to 30.51%, and expenditure on materials and services, ranging from 21.04% to 38.82% of total expenditure, contributed decisively to current spending.
Spending on security services accounted for more than 10% and from 3.92% to 7.20% on groceries.
Spending on accommodation and catering services accounted for a further significant proportion, ranging from 10.82% to 26.96%.
The relatively low share, ranging from 1.03% to 6.25%, was capital spending.
6.2 Shortcomings identified
The results of the audit in the Czech Republic and the Slovak Republic pointed to some similar deficiencies which did not have a major impact on the management of the state's funds and assets.
These were deficiencies in particular in the following areas: (b) breach of the Accounting Act in the field of inventories and failure to comply with the indicative chart of accounts.
In the Czech Republic, minor deficiencies were also found in the disposal of the state's property, where it was left to be used by another person without a proper contract; Failure to comply with the deadline for reporting changes to Land Registry data breached the Land Registry Act; Leaving the waste for disposal to a person who was not the person authorised to handle the waste has infringed the Waste Act.
In addition, deficiencies in the provision of unduly high advances for electricity and natural gas collection, deficiencies in public procurement and in the management of the state's assets were found in the Slovak Republic when its record was not sufficiently secured and the method of disposing of unnecessary assets was not demonstrated.
7. Internal Control System
In the Czech Republic, an internal control system within the meaning of the provisions of Act No 320/2001 Coll., on financial control, was introduced at the SUZ on 1 July 2002, setting out the competencies of the individual employees responsible for carrying out the preliminary, intermediate and follow-up checks.
At the same time, an internal audit system was put in place and an internal auditor of SUZ was appointed.
Each year the SUZ is issued a plan of financial, social and operational technical checks aimed at comprehensively verifying the management of the state's funds and assets in the various asylum facilities managed by the SUZ.
There were 42 management checks in 2002 and 54 in 2003.
In the Slovak Republic, the direct exercise of control activities in the departments and organisations of the Ministry of the Interior of the Slovak Republic in respect of compliance with generally binding legislation and internal regulations is ensured by the Minister's Office of Control.
During the audited period, no check was made on the performance of tasks in the asylum sector.
The Ministry's internal control system is mainly aimed at carrying out preliminary and ongoing financial control according to Act No 502/2001 on financial control and internal audit.
The performance of financial control is also governed by the internal rules of the director of the Ministry.
The material focus of the audit activity as part of the management of the different departments was set out in the annual plan of the IA.
The directors of the union have been designated as responsible for exercising control in this area.
According to the submitted written documentation, no deficiencies were identified.
Since the Decree No 28 of 23 April 2004 of the Ministry of the Interior of the Slovak Republic, the Ministry of the Interior has been obliged to handle and investigate complaints under the Law on Complaints.
The organizational security of this task will need to be completed in the near term.
Communiqué
This report was prepared in Czech, English and Slovak languages and signed by the Vice-President of the Supreme Audit Office and the President of the Najvyššie Audit Office of the Slovak Republic.
Joint Report on Parallel Checks on Highway Construction Expenditure Prague – Dresden
Introduction Word
Without trans-European transport networks, a modern, dynamic and, above all, united Europe would be hard to imagine.
Quality transport networks are a prerequisite for maintaining and raising the standard of living of Europeans.
Creating the conditions for fast, safe, reliable and cheap transport of people and costs is an important task for government.
This is a very demanding task, both organisational and technical and financial.
Europe's efforts at sustainable mobility are often and hard to compromise with Europe's interest in maintaining a healthy and human-friendly environment.
Constructions of transport infrastructure, even with the necessary environmental measures, are financially challenging.
Considerable government procedures and decisions in the preparation and acquisition of transport structures can bring significant savings in public financial resources that can be used in other ways for the benefit of citizens.
One of the roles of the audit institutions is to actively seek deficiencies that have led to lower than achievable economy, efficiency and effectiveness of spending on transport infrastructure construction.
The audit institutions should not only draw the attention of the state administration to the shortcomings identified, but recommend measures to improve the situation, for the benefit of citizens, for the benefit of Europe.
Short Summary
In 2005 and 2006, the two supreme audit institutions of the Federal Republic of Germany and the Czech Republic (hereinafter referred to as the Federal Republic of Germany and the Czech Republic) Bundesrechnungshof (hereinafter referred to as BRH) and the Supreme Audit Office (hereinafter referred to as the SAO) carried out parallel checks on expenditure on the construction of the Prague-Dresden motorway (the D 8 motorway in the Czech Republic and the A 17 motorway in the Federal Republic of Germany).
The highways are connected by a border bridge that is co-financed by both countries.
Thanks to cooperation with the SAO, BRH was able to carry out a thorough check of the effectiveness of the funds spent on the construction of the border bridge.
Based on close cooperation with BRH, the SAO obtained the necessary data on the level of German costs for bridges and tunnels for comparative purposes.
The Czech Republic and the Federal Republic of Germany concluded a contract in 2000 for the construction of a border bridge.
Eventually, according to the construction process, the completion date was scheduled for the end of 2006.
However, during the award procedure, the Directorate of Roads and Highways of the Czech Republic (hereinafter referred to as the RMD) made errors which led to an extension of the competition period and a reduction of the construction time.
The contract with the contractor was thus signed later than anticipated.
The contractor agreed to build the bridge in a shorter time and ensure its completion in 2006.
As a result, however, the RMD had to accept a significant increase in the price of the bridge.
As part of the joint inspection work, bridge prices on the D 8 and A 17 motorways were compared on a per square metre basis for the bridge structure.
Statistical analysis of prices made it possible to identify bridges with high costs per square metre.
Analysis revealed that the border bridge was the most expensive bridge in a group of large bridges with a length of over 100 metres.
The price increase could also be attributed to delays in the procurement process.
When analysing the cost of building the tunnels, the inspectors found that tunnel prices on the D 8 motorway were significantly higher than those of comparable tunnels in the SRN.
BRH and the SAO now have a database on the prices of bridges and tunnels, which they will be able to rely on for future checks on the efficiency of the funds used for transport infrastructure purposes.
For the construction of bridges, both monitoring institutions recommend: standardisation of bridge structures and the use of coupled steel-concrete and special structures only when pre-stressed reinforced concrete bridges are not adequate.
Her team spirit and informality were among the main signs of cooperation.
During the parallel inspection, the controllers shared their specific knowledge and skills.
Introduction
This report reports on the progress and results of the parallel checks of funds spent on the construction of the motorway linking Dresden and Prague (D 8 in the Czech Republic and A 17 in Germany).
The controls were focused on the economy, efficiency and effectiveness of the expenditure incurred.
Parallel inspections were carried out by BRH in Germany and the SAO in the Czech Republic.
Parallel controls took place in 2005 and 2006.
The SAO audited the economy, efficiency and effectiveness of expenditure of approximately CZK 20 billion including VAT (approximately €690 million) on the frontier section of the D 8 motorway, which is numbered 0807.
Of this amount, €64 million of European funds (ISPA) was planned to be used.
In addition to the parallel inspection carried out with the SAO, BRH audited the preparation and construction of the A 17 motorway in 2003 and 2005.
The funds spent on the construction of all three sections of the A 17 motorway amounted to some €670 million, of which €277 million will be co-financed by European funds (EFRE).
The motorway from Prague to Dresden is part of the Trans-European Transport Corridor IV. Dresden - central and southern parts of Eastern Europe - Istanbul.
Highway D 8 runs from Prague through Ústí nad Labem to the state border.
Highway A-17 runs from the Czech Republic/Germany border to Dresden.
Border sections of both motorways were under construction at the time of the inspection.
These sections became operational on 21 December 2006.
On the German side, the motorway is thus completed as far as Dresden, while on the Czech side of highway D 8 it is not completed, as section 0805, covering 16.4 km through the Czech Central Mountains between Řehlovice and Lovosicemi, is still in the preparation phase.
According to the plan, section 0805 between Prague and Ústí nad Labem is to pass through the protected landscape area of České středohoří.
According to forecasts made in the Czech Republic in 2001, there should be 22,940 vehicles per day on the cross-border section 0807 of the D 8 motorway in 2008.
According to German forecasts, the traffic intensity at the Czech border will total about 25,000 vehicles per day in 2010.
In 2000, Germany and the Czech Republic concluded the Treaty between the Federal Republic of Germany and the Czech Republic to link the German motorway A 17 and the Czech motorway D 8 to the common national border by building a border bridge.
Both States are involved in the construction of the bridge, therefore the control of the construction of this bridge was an important aspect of the parallel controls carried out by the two control institutions.
Cooperation of BRH and SAO
Since mid-2005, the two supreme audit institutions have discussed working closely together to control the construction of the motorway linking Prague and Dresden.
Both sides had serious reasons for such cooperation:
1.
The SRN is financially involved in the construction of the border bridge.
Its planning and construction rests with the RMD.
Therefore, BRH itself did not have the opportunity to inspect this construction project.
2.
The SAO did not have the comparative database needed to assess whether the funds had been used cost-effectively in relation to the overall level of costs for bridges and tunnels at D 8.
3.
The SAO needed to ascertain the effectiveness of the expenditure, taking into account the main technical parameters of the motorway, in particular the arrangement of bridges and tunnels, which should allow the same quality of driving on both sides of the border.
Following preparatory negotiations, the agreement on coordinated control of the D 8/A 17 motorway was signed by the presidents of the two supreme audit institutions on 4 January 2006.
The joint working meetings of the BRH auditors and the SAO took place over the whole of 2006 at an interval of approximately six to eight weeks.
The meetings addressed current issues and challenges, in particular the preparation of a uniform methodology for controlling the prices of bridges and tunnels, the exchange of findings from the inspection of the construction of the border bridge and the preparation of joint reports.
The continuous exchange of information was a major sign of cooperation.
While BRH did most of the work on comparing the technical parameters of bridges and tunnels and tendering procedures, the SAO largely prepared a methodology for controlling the prices of bridges and tunnels.
The applicable legal standards for government control differ in the way control actions are closed in the two countries.
For these reasons, the two supreme audit institutions drew their own control conclusions on their findings, obtained during their inspections.
This report reflects the results achieved through parallel controls.
The cooperation will end with the signing of the final communiqué in the first half of 2007.
Border bridge construction
The Czech Republic and the Federal Republic of Germany agreed in a 2000 contract on the completion date of the construction of the border bridge, as far as possible, in 2005.
The two sides then agreed to postpone this date until the end of 2006.
The RMD acted as investor.
The SRN is financially involved in building the bridge out of 42.13%.
In the period up to 1999, the RMD proposed that the border bridge should have a pre-stressed reinforced concrete structure.
In 1999, in agreement with its partners in Germany, the RMD agreed to a bonded steel-concrete structure.
This decision was justified by the reduction of the number of twin pillars from seven to five and the subsequent environmental benefits of such a solution for the valley.
In addition, comparative calculations carried out by the RMD indicated that the braced steel-concrete structure should be a less expensive option.
But there was a mistake in the calculations.
They stated that the new fused steel-concrete structure would be only 397m long.
In fact, it's 5% longer.
During the award of the contract for the border bridge, the RMD made serious errors.
It has not prepared contract documentation that adequately describes the assessment criteria and sub-criteria for the comparison of tenders.
This error resulted in the bid evaluation and evaluation panel and the Director-General evaluating the bids in a subjective and problematic manner.
The unsuccessful tenderer contested the results of the contract procedure.
Subsequent investigations by the Competition Authority led to the contract for the construction contract being signed later than initially calculated.
This meant that the 2004 summer construction season, following the evaluation of the bids, was wasted and the time allocated to construction was reduced.
The construction company agreed to build the bridge in less time, but at a substantial cost increase.
The process offered included the double installation of a bridge top, a change in the structure of the pillars and the establishment and fitting-out of mounting spaces.
In addition, the construction company was unable to procure the type of steel originally proposed by the required deadline.
The new type of steel – at a cost of some €4 million more, which had to be paid in full by the RMD – allowed a reduction in the time taken to implement anti-corrosion protection, which in turn enabled the overall construction time of the border bridge to be reduced.
Compared to the net price of €18.3 million offered in the tender, the total cost of building the border bridge will increase considerably as a result of the accelerated procedure and other adjustments in financing.
The reduced time allocated to construction work led to a price increase of about €5.9 million.
At present, the net cost amount is around €25.9 million, not taking into account automatic price increases (as of October 2006).
Comparison of bridge and tunnel prices
The two supreme audit institutions carried out an analysis of the prices of bridges on the D 8 and A 17 motorways in order to identify bridges that were priced too high and to compare the prices of bridges on the German and Czech sides.
The analysis relied on methods of mathematical statistics.
Furthermore, tunnels on D 8 were compared with similar tunnels on the SRN motorways (A 71, A 38 and A 4).
There are no similar tunnels on the A 17.
In preparing this part of the inspection, it was important to accurately identify the relevant prices.
The two supreme audit institutions have established the following principles for comparing bridge prices: based on the actual price paid for the bridge, excluding value added tax (VAT rates vary and are subject to change in the Czech Republic and SNR); do not take into account prices for the special foundations of the bridge; convert prices in CZK into € at the European Central Bank rate applicable on 30.12.2005 (CZK 29 = € 1).
Both D 8 and A 17 have bridges of different designs (steel-concrete, pre-stressed reinforced concrete, joined-together steel-concrete and steel) and different sizes.
In order to ensure the homogeneity of the structures examined, the bridges were divided into groups according to the type of construction.
Within these groups, the size of the bridge was also taken into account in the analyses.
By far the most common type of construction was concrete bridges on both sides of the boundary (20 bridges on D 8, 36 bridges on A 17).
The analysis concentrated mainly on this group of bridges, as the size of the population allowed the use of mathematical-statistical methods with a high degree of reliability of results.
Checks found that prices per m 2 of concrete bridges on both D 8 and A 17 depend on the size of the bridge (length, area): the larger the bridge, the lower the price per m2 of bridge construction.
For German bridges, the price's dependence on size was stronger than for Czech bridges (see charts below).
Two concrete bridges at A 17 and four at D 8 have been identified and are priced significantly higher.
A method to detect atypical values in a set of numbers was used to locate these bridges, as well as a regression analysis taking into account the bridge price's dependence on its size (for determining atypically expensive bridges, the standard distance from the regression curve is upwards).
However, information about the atypically high price of those bridges should only be seen as an indication of which bridges need to be targeted.
In order to draw a generally applicable conclusion, the causes of the high price in each case identified should be analysed.
One such cause may be inefficient administrative action, or environmental requirements or some other plausible reason.
Given that Czech concrete bridges are on average longer than German ones (see table below), the proven negative dependence of the bridge price on its length could lead to the assumption that Czech bridges should be cheaper.
The hypothesis of lower prices for Czech concrete bridges is also supported by lower labour costs in the Czech construction sector compared to the SRN.
On the contrary, the results of the analysis show that the prices of concrete bridges at D 8 are higher than at A 17.
Two possible causes for this have been identified during the joint negotiations:
1.
In Germany, most bridges are awarded as separate contracts.
Such an award allows small and medium-sized regional firms to take part in the tender.
