In the Czech Republic, many bridges are awarded as part of a contract for part of the motorway route, which is several kilometres long.
Small and medium-sized companies do not participate in such a competition because the contract is too big for them.
2.
There is more variability in bridge designs in the CR than in the SRN.
In this context, the SAO found that the type of bridge construction is decided long before its construction.
The decision is taken at the time when the RMD submits an application for a territorial decision.
Although the RMD selects bridge types from several variants at this time, economy plays a minor role in decision-making.
A general conclusion on the prices of bridges in the Czech Republic and Germany could only be reached on the basis of a thorough analysis of the construction works market in both countries, which would, however, go beyond this control action.
The border bridge is the most expensive coupled ocelobeton bridge at D 8 and A 17.
In the case of tunnels, it was found that tunnel prices on the D 8 are twice as high as tunnels in the SRN.
However, a comparison of the main construction-technical parameters of the tunnels on the D 8 with the tunnels in the SRN showed no significant difference.
Significant price differences could be attributed to the fact that there is no real competition in the tunnel construction industry in the Czech Republic.
However, the small number of tunnels did not allow general conclusions to be drawn.
Effectiveness and efficiency of the construction of the Dresden-Prague motorway
A separate CR was created in 1993.
In the same year, the government approved the concept of development of the motorway network.
The D 8 motorway was to be gradually extended from Prague to the national border with Germany.
Section No 0805 through the Czech Central Mountains was to be acquired before the border section No 0807.
The Czech Republic acquires the D 8 motorway under programme financing.
In programme No 327 220 ‘Construction of the D 8 motorway Prague – Ústí nad Labem – state border of the Czech Republic/SRN” set two objectives for the construction of the D 8 motorway:
1.
improve the connection between Prague and Ústí nad Labem – a national objective;
2.
improve the connection between Prague and Berlin within the framework of the IV Trans-European Transport Corridor Berlin-Istanbul - an international objective.
According to the programme, the construction of the section leading through the Czech Central Mountains was to be completed in June 2008 and the construction of the border section No 0807 in June 2007.
It should be added here that the completion date is not identical to the commissioning date.
The SAO found that the preparation of the construction of the section leading through the Czech Central Mountains is now at the stage of an official territorial decision.
Environmental activists have challenged the decision with lawsuits.
There is no final decision on the claims.
It can therefore not be excluded that the section running through the Czech Central Mountains will be completed several years later than indicated in the documentation of the programme.
In the summer of 2006, the Czech Ministry of Transport proposed to postpone the date of completion of section 0805 until 2010.
As a result of these facts, the construction of the cross-border section has not achieved the national objective of improving the link between Prague and Ústí nad Labem.
The RMD made an estimate of capital expenditure for the border section D 8 No 0807 and section No 0805 through the Czech Central Mountains in 1999-2001.
The estimate in these capital expenditure included investment costs is about €639 million combined for both Sections Nos 0805 and 0807.
Table 2: Economic Assessment Results (EIRR = Internal Rate of Yield)
All motorways and expressways under consideration open, both sections of D 8 opened in 2007.
On the northern part of the border section, the traffic intensity of 18,000 vehicles a day in 2010
All motorways and expressways under consideration open, both sections of D 8 opened in 2007.
On the northern part of the border section, the traffic intensity of 22,000 vehicles a day in 2010
All motorways and expressways under consideration open, both sections of D 8 opened in 2007.
On the northern part of the border section, the traffic intensity of 25,000 vehicles a day in 2010
An economic analysis carried out in 2001 was based on the assumption that the border section and the section through the Czech Central Mountains would be completed in time.
However, at the time of the inspection, it is clear that the section through the Czech Central Mountains will not be opened at the original date.
This will reduce the aforementioned EIRRs.
The cost of both sections, net of VAT, as at 31.12.2005, was €897 million, i.e. higher by 27.4% than the cost level used in the economic analysis in 2001.
However, there is a risk that section 0805 will be run on a different, more expensive route, which would affect the aforementioned threshold of economic viability.
Economic analysis has shown that the construction of both sections of the motorway is economically viable at a traffic intensity of at least 18,000 vehicles per day in 2010.
The forecast for 2008 was 22,940 vehicles, but a study addressing the issue of bypass routes for the missing section across the Czech Central Highlands calculates a traffic intensity of 12,600 to 13,750 vehicles per day, pushing the investment below the threshold of economic viability.
With the higher load of commuter routes for the missing section through the Czech Central Mountains, there is a high probability of congestion and thus an increase in external costs, which was not foreseen in the original economic analysis.
The above mentioned reduction in revenues and increase in costs means that the already low EIRR that the construction of the D 8 border section should have achieved will not be achieved.
Construction of the D 8 border section is therefore inefficient.
The construction of the A 17 motorway also involved similar cost overruns to those of the two sections on the D 8.
As part of the overall economic assessment of the 1992 Federal Road Development Plan for Long-haul Transport, the Federal Ministry of Transport of Germany projected a cost of about €320 million.
The resulting expense-income ratio was then 4.6.
In the meantime, however, costs have risen sharply to about €670 million, and the cost-benefit ratio has more than halved.
In addition to the parallel inspection carried out jointly with the SAO, BRH audited the preparation and construction of the A 17 motorway in 2003 and 2005.
In doing so, BRH found that the relevant State Approval Authority had required an off-road tunnel for Section A 17, third section, which was not needed.
In the course of the inspection, BRH managed to convince the Federal Ministry and the German road building administration that the tunnel was not necessary.
This resulted in savings that amounted to around €10 million in construction costs alone and €0.15 million in annual operating costs.
Assessing the first section of A 17, BRH found that the construction project had not been properly prepared.
The following example illustrates this:
At the preparatory stage, the Road Construction Authority did not investigate the conditions of the bedrock in the Dölzschen rocks because the site was difficult to access.
This was done only after the tunnel stamping machine had struck the rock.
Tests showed that the rocks were more weathered inside than anticipated during the selection process.
Costly measures such as anchoring, injections and stone-falling protective nets, which cost about €3.3 million, therefore had to be taken.
The Road and Motorway Directorate had to bear the costs not only of other extraordinary operations, such as safety measures in the rocks of Dölzschen, but also of measures that would not have been needed if the construction project had been properly prepared first.
Expenditure of this kind includes costs related to the interruption of construction operations (€3.2 million), additional training for drilling staff (€0.5 million) and rising wages and expenditure on goods due to project overruns (€7 million).
The cost overruns thus amounted to some €10.7 million, which was not matched by any value added.
Based on the lessons learned from this and other audited projects, BRH asked the Federal Ministry to apply stricter guidance in its supervision of road construction works.
In doing so, the Federal Ministry should give the Directorates of Roads and Highways the necessary incentive to introduce quality assurance systems.
Benefits of cooperation for BRH and SAO Cooperation fulfilled the purpose of both audit institutions.
BRH gained control knowledge of the preparation, assignment and progress of the construction of the border bridge.
The two supreme audit institutions have built a database of bridge and tunnel prices on which future inspections can rely.
The SAO found that the main construction-technical parameters of bridges, tunnels and route of the D 8 motorway do not differ from those of the A 17.
The auditors of the two institutions involved in the cooperation have gained new experience, which they can use for further inspections.

Coordinated control of funds earmarked for financing projects implemented under the Cross-Border Cooperation Operational Programme Czech Republic - Republic of Poland 2007-2013
INTRODUCTION
The evolving community of the people of Europe is a driving force behind cross-border cooperation as an integral part of the wider territorial cooperation that has been part of our lives for the last 20 years.
So let this community provide the opening word on this joint report by the two supreme audit institutions of neighbouring countries:
"185 million EU citizens (37% of the population) live in border areas.
These areas are usually underdeveloped and peripheral territories, often relics of darker parts of Europe's history.
The main objective of cross-border cooperation is to help alleviate the existence of borders as administrative, legal and physical barriers, and to further help develop the dormant potential of border regions.
The programmes cover different border regions: some cover sea basins, others inland borders within the EU or borders shared with candidate countries.
Most programmes are bilateral, but some involve more than two neighbouring countries. "
The programmes last for seven years and receive a total of EUR 5.7 billion from the EU budget.
"The programmes include the following activities: repairing and reconstructing cross-border roads, cycling paths and bridges, investing in cross-border waste management systems, health facilities, research centres, flood prevention measures, etc., joint management of natural sites or development of tourist destinations, development of joint services for the local population, advice on employment issues, creation of thematic networks and clusters for innovation.
Support from the programme is provided on condition that organisations from both sides of the border (such as regional authorities, universities or SMEs) come together to implement projects that are based on the needs of the border region.
Representatives of cooperating regions, relevant ministries and other local partners meet regularly to decide which projects will be supported.
This system should lead to a sustainable form of cooperation even after the programme itself has come to an end.
Funding is provided on condition that project partners from both sides of the border work together in a way that fulfils at least two of the following four features: joint preparation, joint implementation, joint staff, joint financing. "
Within the partnership, the tasks and responsibilities for the preparation, implementation, financing and control of project activities are clearly defined and distributed to the relevant partners.
Key attributes of the partnership include: joint choice of lead partner, joint project preparation and application, conclusion of a joint partnership agreement signed by all partners, taking overall responsibility for project implementation by the lead partner, overall support payments being accepted by the lead partner and transferred to the respective project partners.
Eligible projects carried out on the territory of the districts and voivodships adjacent to the common border may be supported.
OBJECTIVES AND SCOPE OF CONTROL
On 11 December 2007, the European Commission (EC) approved the Operational Programme for Cross-Border Cooperation Czech Republic-Republic of Poland 2007-2013 (OPPS ČR-POL).
€258,187,467 has been earmarked for the implementation of the operational programme.
The total included a contribution from European funds (ERDF) totalling €219,459,344 (85%), which must be spent by the end of 2015.
Financial data on the uptake of EU funds from the Cross-Border Cooperation Operational Programme Czech Republic-Republic of Poland 2007-2013 at the end of 2013 are presented in the table below:
The agreement on cooperation between the supreme audit institutions of the Czech Republic (SAO) and the Republic of Poland (NIK) was concluded by the presidents of both authorities on 27 February 2013, following previous mutual meetings at the meeting of the heads of supreme audit institutions of the Visegrad Four, Austria and Slovenia, which took place in Lovasberény, Hungary, in September 2012.
The aim of the cooperation was to check that the implementation of the operational programme was carried out in accordance with the legislation and with binding programming documents or agreements, that the management and control system was functioning reliably, that the objectives of the operational programme were being achieved and that the projects selected were being implemented in accordance with the conditions laid down for the aid granted.
This co-operation took the form of a parallel control, in which the supervisory authorities, on the basis of their legal mandate, independently examined topics the content of which had been jointly discussed beforehand.
THISsegmentISintentionallyLEFTblank
The audit carried out also included elements of a performance audit.
The selected topics were examined on a sample of projects with project partners on both sides of the border.
The checks carried out resulted in audit findings published in the usual way by both authorities.
This joint report is based on the two control conclusions set out in the Annex.
The selected common control sample included six projects (Annex 1) which fall within the support area 1.1 — Strengthening transport accessibility and the support area 2.2 — Promoting tourism development.
Prior to the start of the audit, the uptake of funds from these two areas of support represented more than 60% of the funds reimbursed to beneficiaries by the end of 2012 from the ERDF for all projects implemented under the entire OPPS ČR–POL.
THISsegmentISintentionallyLEFTblank
In addition to the control of a common sample of six projects, the SAO audited a further 13 projects and NIK audited two others (all from support areas 1.1 and 2.2).
The funds paid to beneficiaries of the ERDF, which were audited by the SAO and NIK, totalled €25,362,174 (SAO) and €8,450,784 (NIK).
The audited volume represented 41% of all funds paid to beneficiaries from aid areas 1.1 and 2.2.
MAIN CONTROL CONCLUSIONS
Both inspections carried out independently by the SAO on the Czech side and NIK on the Polish side showed that the level of irregularities identified did not exceed the level of materiality.
Implementation of OPPS ČR–POL, covering the operation of the management and control system and the implementation of selected projects, was successful.
The auditees who were part of the management and control system took steps to limit the risk of misuse of funds and under-use of ERDF funds.
Proof of the effectiveness of the projects implemented under the operational programme is the high level of achievement of the objectives set, which was also supported by the results of the survey of the social evaluation of project implementation.
SUMMARY OF CONTROL RESULTS
A. Evaluation of management and control system
The coordinated approach made it possible to carry out a check of the OPPS ČR–POL management and control system implemented by the following institutions in both countries: the managing authority (Ministry for Local Development of the Czech Republic), the national coordinator (Ministry for Local Development POL) and the controllers (Centre for Regional Development and Voivodship).
At the same time, the coordinated audit allowed for the evaluation of the tasks and functions carried out by the Joint Technical Secretariat and the Monitoring Committee.
During the evaluation of the management system, the audit focused on the activities carried out in relation to the set-up and performance of the functions of the managing authority and the national coordinator, in particular in relation to the setting of the objectives of the programme and the monitoring of the progress made, the evaluation activities and annual reports, the assessment, evaluation, selection and approval of project applications, the absorption of programme funds and the fulfilment of the obligation to publish correct, complete and up-to-date information on subsidies, the control activities of the managing authority, the set-up of the performance of activities and outputs from the audits of the entrusted body and the system for reporting and monitoring irregularities and compliance with it, while the coordination of the OPL was examined.
Furthermore, the activities of the competent authorities in the area of risk analysis and establishment of control plans at the site of project execution were examined and evaluations of project applications carried out by independent evaluators were assessed.
The examination of the control system included a review of the administrative and on-the-spot checks that allowed the approval of e.g. supplies of goods and co-financed services, the eligibility of expenditure submitted for reimbursement, while the compliance of the disbursement of these funds and related activities (or parts thereof) with national and EU legislation.
Significant shortcomings were found by the SAO audit in the publication of correct, complete and up-to-date information on the subsidies granted.
In the area of project evaluation and approval, a risk of non-transparency of the process has been identified.
Other weaknesses were found in the setting of monitoring indicators and in the setting of the reporting system for irregularities.
Based on these findings, the Supreme Audit Office assessed the control and management system implemented by the managing authority as partially effective.
The irregularities identified by NIK, which related to the operation of the management and control system in Poland in relation to violations of applicable laws and rules, were not assessed as significant.
These irregularities only had an impact on individual projects and did not have a significant impact on the implementation of the entire OPPS CR-POL.
Despite the identified discrepancies, NIK assessed the functioning of the system positively.
B. Evaluation of project results
The audit did not show a discrepancy between the indicators and targets set for individual projects and those defined by the operational programme.
In all the projects audited, the expected results were achieved, the funded activities were implemented and the indicators (measurable parameters) set out in the project applications of the operational programme were met.
The audit conclusions are in line with the outcome of the Social Assessment of Project Implementation survey described below.
An important aspect that the SAO and NIK paid attention to in their conclusions is the limited possibility of an objective evaluation of the performance of the anticipated results of individual projects, taking into account both the objectives of the individual projects and the objectives of the entire operational programme.
On initiative The Supreme Audit Chamber conducted an opinion poll conducted in the summer of 2013 at the site of the implementation of projects selected for the common control sample.
The survey and its evaluation were carried out by the School of Higher Vocational Education in Nysa (Poland).
In agreement with the Supreme Audit Office, the survey was carried out on both sides of the border between 2,820 respondents, both to the public in Poland in relation to the part of the project carried out by Polish project partners and to the Czech public in relation to the part of the project carried out in the Czech Republic.
The survey covered in particular the suitability, quality, exchange of information, use of funds and social function of projects including investments made.
The number of respondents per project is given in Annex 2.
An assessment of the survey responses indicated that the overall social rating of the projects was good, reaching 4.8 points (on a scale with a maximum of 8 points), meaning that project results were viewed positively by the majority of respondents both in terms of objectives and other parameters mentioned in the project applications.
According to respondents, the projects examined fulfilled the criteria expressed in the operational programme.
Respondents also noted the positive impact of investments in improving the quality of transport and tourism infrastructure, transport accessibility, tourist attractiveness, business conditions, quality of life of citizens and re-establishing contacts between residents of the border region.
On the other hand, the overall assessment of responses indicated a certain difference between the actual level of achievement of objectives/parameters (actual impact rate) and the level expected by respondents.
This difference applies to the evaluation of the results of individual projects, where in some cases there is a significant difference.
The results of the survey were in line with the views of the participants in the panel discussions (focus groups) carried out as part of NIK's control on the Polish side, who pointed to the need to implement the projects and confirmed their positive public acceptance and delivery of the expected results.
C. Evaluation of legality of audited projects
The beneficiaries were checked for the consistency of the substantive and time execution of the project with the project application schedule and content, the fulfilment of the set objectives and purpose of the project, the fulfilment of mandatory monitoring indicators, the management of changes in the projects, the handling of potential project revenues, the fulfilment of the obligation to keep a separate accounting record of project transactions, compliance with the ban on project funding from other EU sources, the implementation of the project partnership and compliance with its terms and conditions, the fulfilment of mandatory publicity relating in particular to the dissemination of the sources of EU project funding, compliance with the conditions for eligibility of project expenditure, the selection of contractors and the verification of deliverables delivered.
The total financial value of the deficiencies qualified by the SAO and NIK as expenditure ineligible for EU funding was found to be €85,094.
However, that amount did not exceed the established level of materiality relating to the total audited volume of funds for the sample of projects.
Despite the irregularities found, the SAO and NIK evaluated the implementation of the selected projects positively by the beneficiaries.
In most cases, commitments under the terms of the aid were duly met by the beneficiaries.
The funds intended for the implementation of the audited projects were spent in accordance with EU regulations and national law.
However, seven of the eight beneficiaries audited by NIK were found to be irregular in the implementation of substantive and financial obligations.
These were individual cases of a non-systemic nature and concerned particularly inconsistent preparation of project documentation and breaches of public procurement rules, but did not affect the choice of contractor or the delivery of contractually agreed works.
In only one of all projects audited by NIK, an irregularity found to relate to non-compliance with reporting obligations may qualify as ineligible expenditure, amounting to PLN 43 003 (of which €8 231 funded by the ERDF).
On the other side of the border, the SAO identified ineligible expenditure of CZK 2 098 053 in three of the 19 audited projects (of which €76 863 was funded by the ERDF).
Irregularities included a failure to deliver the materials and works paid for or an expense for the modernisation of a property owned by a person who was not linked to the project either as a beneficiary of the aid or as a project partner.
RECOMMENDATIONS
The joint recommendation of the SAO and NIK concerns the improvement of the monitoring system in particular with regard to the consistent setting of proper monitoring indicators for all specific objectives of the priority axis of the operational programme.
In general, efforts must also be made to establish such indicators at both programme and project level that allow more reliable verification of the implementation of the identified impacts.
Comments and recommendations from checks carried out on both sides of the border are included in separate audit conclusions of the SAO and NIK on the CD.
COMMUNICATES
The report was drawn up in Czech, English and Polish languages and signed by the presidents of the supreme audit institutions of the Czech Republic and the Republic of Poland.
Public works procurement and corruption prevention
Joint report on parallel audit by Czech Supreme Audit Office (SAO) and German Federal Court of Auditors (BRH)
Introduction and Overview
Procurement of goods and services by contracting authorities is very important for both the state and private sectors.
The role of the state is to use public resources as efficiently and cost-effectively as possible and to ensure fair and regulated free competition.
In the European Union (EU), public procurement is a key factor for the success of the single economic area.
In its regulations, the EU has set limits above which contracting authorities must meet certain requirements to guarantee a broad and unrestricted competitive environment across the EU.
EU Member States must therefore transpose European procurement law into their national legislation and put it into practice.
Both the Czech Republic and Germany have already done so.
For public contracts below EU thresholds, the regulation of their award remains the responsibility of individual countries.
Moreover, public procurement is vulnerable to corruption, with consequent high potential for harm.
Therefore, contracting authorities are obliged to take reasonable measures in their procurement procedures to avoid corruption and unfair favouritism (prevention of corruption).
In January 2011, the Supreme Audit Office of the Czech Republic (Czech SAI - NCA) and the Federal Court of Auditors of Germany (German SAI - BRH) agreed to conduct parallel checks in the field of procurement procedures and prevention of corruption in land and transport structures.
Cooperation was also focused on the field of European procurement law as transposed into national legislation.
Controls included awarding contracts below European limits in order to crack down on corruption prevention.
The inspection findings of the two supreme audit institutions are summarised in a joint report addressed, inter alia, to international organisations such as the International Organisation of Supreme Audit Institutions (INTOSAI).
Areas of control and audited institutions
The BRH assessment is based on blanket inspections of the above-mentioned areas, which were carried out at the German construction administrations in charge of federal transport and land structures between 2011 and 2012.
For example, motorway works and large-scale building projects were assessed.
BRH included findings from past inspections in the evaluation.
The SAO's assessment is based on inspections carried out during the years 2008 to 2012, which focused on construction of roads, railway lines, water projects, large buildings (hospitals, cultural facilities, universities) and some other buildings in the Czech Republic.
In Germany, federal procurement is mostly carried out by the Land's building authorities.
In the Czech Republic, the state institutions for road and rail construction are responsible for the procurement of transport infrastructure contracts.
In the field of building, procurement is carried out by entities benefiting from budget funds.
In both countries, the services of external experts are also used at each stage of the procurement process.
The evaluation must take into account the differences in procurement legislation and the specificities of the construction market in both countries.
Structure of public procurement law in both countries
In Germany, the conduct of European procurement procedures is governed by laws and sub-statutory standards.
Procurement procedures limited to Germany shall be governed by budgetary law and relevant administrative acts .
The internal administrative rules contain numerous additional provisions for both European and national procurement procedures.
Further details on the requirements of European procurement law and the prevention of corruption are set out in the federal government's so-called procurement manuals, in which the requirements are elaborated or formulated in greater detail, taking into account legal precedents in the field of procurement law.
In the Czech Republic, public procurement is governed by the Public Procurement Act and other legislation concerning contracts with a value above and below European limits.
The procurement law was substantially changed in 2012.
The new rules have strengthened the transparency of procurement procedures, opened up scope for more competition and reduced opportunities for corruption during the procurement process.
Essential aspects of procurement law in both countries
As far as European procurement procedures are concerned, open and narrower procedures in particular need to be applied in both countries.
In Germany, contracts below the European limit are awarded in accordance with national rules (public call for competition, restricted call for competition, negotiated procedures), but these are practically identical to the above-mentioned conditions for European procurement procedures.
Public procurement in Germany is characterised by the principle of preference for open procedures (where candidates on a European scale are invited) and public calls for participation (only in the case of national procedures).
In open procedures and when invited to take part in a contest, a tender may be submitted by any interested party.
Another important feature of German procurement law is the support of small and medium-sized firms.
This is achieved primarily by dividing the total procurement into business-specific lots and dividing into sub-contracts.
The award of contracts divided into these lots gives as many candidates as possible a chance to participate in the procedure.
The two characteristics of German procurement law are time-tested tools for getting good value for money.
There are more than 330,000 construction companies registered in Germany with 1.9 million employees.
In the Czech Republic, the terms of European procedures must be applied even in cases of public procurement with a value below the EU limit.
The exception is the simplified sub-standard procedure, where the contracting authority can apply less onerous procedures.
In accordance with European law, Czech procurement law includes the conditions under which the contract must be awarded in order to ensure competition and achieve a favourable price.
Current Czech legislation allows contracting authorities to choose between an open procedure and a narrower procedure.
There are 85,000 construction companies registered in the Czech Republic, of which about 1,900 have 20 or more employees.
Total procurement volume of public works by contracting authorities and proportion of each type of procedure in both countries
In 2011, the total volume of construction works in Germany amounted to EUR 7 675 billion.
CZK 1,138 billion (15%) of which was the share of public contracts that included contracts awarded by individual Länder and municipalities, in addition to those awarded by the federal government.
The total volume of federal road and land construction projects (excluding construction of railways and water structures) amounted to CZK 212.5 billion.
In 2011, the composition of the federal land works and transport works contracts was as follows: 80,7 % open procedures and public calls; 12.7% narrower governance and challenges;
6,6 % negotiated procedures and direct awards; 33.2% is the proportion of European procurement procedures.
The volume of public construction contracts in the Czech Republic reached 118.6 billion CZK in 2011, which was about 25% of the total volume of all construction works.
In 2009 it reached CZK 194.0 billion, which was 37% of the total.
Structure of procurement types in 2011: 65.1% open procedures; 14,3 % restricted management; 9,5 % negotiated procedure; 8,4 % simplified procedures for sub-standard contracts; 2.7% other management.
The figures include all supply, service and works contracts awarded by contracting authorities in 2011.
These figures show that: parallel controls in both countries targeted a substantial proportion of public procurement procedures; prevention of corruption, which must be kept in mind in procurement procedures, has a significant financial impact; open significance. procurement procedures are of key importance.
Summary of main results
The SAO and BRH carried out parallel checks on the award of European works contracts, with an emphasis on preventing corruption in their countries.
The inspections focused on comparing legislation in the two countries and the EU and on the implementation of land and transport procurement contracts.
The following are the key findings:
The EU Public Procurement Directive has been transposed into the national laws of both countries (points 1 and 3.1).
Preventing corruption is not only the aim of the EU directive, but of the two countries' national procurement rules, which differ in part in both focus and detail.
In both countries, the basic requirement that tender documents must meet is to include all the information needed to prepare tenders.
The specification shall be neutral and shall not contain any requirements restricting competition.
Tender documents in Germany generally meet these requirements.
In the Czech Republic, the problem is the lack of definition of the subject matter of the contract.
In Germany, splitting contracts into lots is required where possible in order to promote a competitive environment.
According to BRH's findings, this requirement is respected in Germany.
The SAO's inspections showed that in the Czech Republic considerable public works in the field of roadworks are commissioned without being divided into parts or being divided, but only into very significant parts in volume. This may distort competition and lead to restrictions on only a few large companies.
These limitations on market potential can result in significant cost increases and create the risk of non-serious price negotiations and impermissible conventions.
Legislation in both countries allows contracting authorities to award additional works under certain conditions if the need arises after the contract has been concluded (point 4.1).
In both countries, the requirements for correctly determining the type of procurement procedure are met.
In the Czech Republic, under the applicable regulations, contracting authorities are free to designate one of the open procurement procedures, namely an open procedure or a restricted procedure.
German procurement and budgetary law enshrines the primacy of open procedures for both European tenures and national ones, known as public calls for competition.
Open tender procedures in both countries allow a larger range of candidates to submit a bid and also reduce the risk of corruption by making it more difficult to collude illegally between bidders.
At the same time, the best results in terms of value for money are being achieved.
In the case of large contracts for which the estimated total price reaches or exceeds the limits set by the EU, the audited contracting authorities always launched a European procurement procedure.
Correct determination of the estimated contract value plays a very important role in determining the type of procurement procedure.
In Germany, databases are used to determine the price of public works objectively, in which values are based on completed construction projects.
This is a sufficiently objective tool.
In the Czech Republic, databases are used only in the area of road construction, but are not yet an effective tool (point 4.2).
When awarding a works contract under European procurement procedures, prior information and contract notices need to be published in order to enhance competition by allowing candidates to prepare in time for the submission of tenders.
As a rule, the construction authorities audited complied with this requirement.
In isolated cases, errors in the information released, incorrect application of provisions and/or a lack of controls may have impaired competition (item 4.3).
There is a greater risk of manipulation when selecting candidates to participate in a restricted procurement procedure.
Under EU law, the number of candidates invited must be high enough to guarantee genuine competition.
Selection criteria shall be objective, non-discriminatory and published sufficiently in advance.
Beyond EU requirements, contracting authorities in both countries should as often as possible rotate candidates who invite participation in the procedure when selecting suppliers.
In Germany, there are in part very detailed requirements to prevent the risks of manipulation.
In some cases, the audited contracting authorities did not follow these requirements.
In the Czech Republic, selection decisions were not always credible (point 4.4).
In both countries, contracting authorities must ensure during the procurement process that all candidates wishing to submit a tender receive the same information.
Any agreements between these candidates, and between them and third parties involved in the preparation of the specifications, must be avoided as far as possible, so as not to restrict competition through unlawful agreements.
In Germany, the measures are more thorough than in the Czech Republic.
However, the audited German contracting authorities did not meet this requirement in several cases (point 4.5).
In both countries, the principle of cross-checking and transparency must be applied when opening envelopes.
Labelling, documentation, protection and confidentiality requirements are intended to limit the scope for manipulation and are particularly detailed in Germany.
Checks in both countries found only a few cases of irregularities (paragraph 4.6).
The assessment and evaluation of bids is regulated procedurally in both countries.
The relevant German measures are more comprehensive, but have not always been respected by the audited contracting authorities.
For example, contracting authorities passed on their tenders to third parties for inspection without prior assessment, without properly documenting any suspicious circumstances.
Thus, offers were insufficiently protected against subsequent manipulation (paragraph 4.7).
For the purposes of legal protection, the participants must be informed about the intended contract award before the latter is finalised.
This legal protection rule also is designed to prevent corruptive action.
The law of the Czech Republic provides for judicial review of contract award procedures also for national tenders.
Only in few cases, the notification of the enterprises was not appropriately ensured (item 4.8).
The tender documentation was not always complete or fully credible for the audited contracting entities in both countries.
In the case of several procurement procedures, transparency requirements applied at European level were not always properly respected in the minutes of the procedure.
In addition, some of the weaknesses identified reduced the preventive anti-corruption function of the dossier.
Contracting entities in both countries mostly complied with their reporting obligations (paragraph 4.9).
Summary of the main principles of European procurement law and the prevention of corruption
European tender law
In order to ensure free competition in the EU, the European Parliament and the Council of the European Union adopted, inter alia, a Directive on the coordination of procedures for the award of public works contracts, public supply contracts and public service contracts to ensure the free movement of goods and services, and a Directive on the coordination of laws, regulations and administrative provisions relating to review procedures for the award of public supply contracts and public works contracts (the Remedies Directive), both of which were to be transposed into national law.
The general EU procurement principles include in particular: a requirement for a competitive environment; a requirement for equal treatment or non-discrimination; a requirement for transparency.
EU procurement principles include the Directive on the Coordination of Public Procurement Procedures in addition includes provisions for construction projects with a total foreseeable value of over €5 million.
These provisions shall affect: the content of the procurement documents; type of procurement procedure used; disclosure of certain information before, during and after proceedings; compliance with the deadline for the receipt of tenders; the qualification requirements that may be required of tenderers; the criteria for selecting the most suitable tender.
The legal protection of candidates is governed by the Remedies Directive.
In the event of a potential breach of procurement law, tenderers may have the contracting authority's decision reviewed in accordance with the review procedures.
Both countries transposed EU requirements into national legislation.
Preventing corruption
The importance of preventing corruption
There is no legal definition in either country of what corruption actually means.
Corruption is widely understood as an abuse of a personal (confidential) position for its own benefit.
As a rule, this means that such abuse is encouraged by those benefiting.
Corruption can be an obstacle to competition and can cause serious economic losses.
Total corruption-related damages are estimated to account for 5% of the world's economic output.
For the EU, these costs are estimated at 1% of the EU's economic output.
Where a contracting authority is involved, agreements restricting competition or allowing dishonest conduct may undermine confidence in the integrity, ability and overall work of the public administration.
That is why preventing corruption is an essential task for contracting authorities.
Practice and procedures must be scrutinised, particularly in areas particularly vulnerable to corruption, so that adequate measures can be taken to prevent it.
Taking preventive action and carrying out effective controls can substantially reduce the susceptibility of the decision-making process to corruption.
As a rule, the award of works contracts is considered to be an area particularly vulnerable to corruption.
Measures to prevent corruption
In Germany, corruption is prosecutable under criminal and labour law, with particular regard to active and passive bribery, receiving and offering significant benefits.
The German federal government has issued a manual of recommendations on the prevention of corruption in the federal administration, according to which the following principles are applied throughout the procurement process outside of risk analysis: preference for open procurement; principle of multiple control and transparent decision-making; separation of planning, procurement and accounting phases; accurate and complete tender documentation.
In addition, other measures having a preventive effect against corruption have become part of procurement law.
In 2011, the relevant Federal Ministry of Information Technology introduced a system to monitor procurement for the construction of federal roads.
Using this system, data such as estimated costs, total contract value and the involvement of private design offices can be fully recorded and analysed to identify any discrepancies.
In the Czech Republic, corruption is prosecuted under criminal law, and the level of labour or civil law is also an option.
The legal requirement for transparency, equal treatment and non-discrimination throughout the procurement process (preparation and implementation) is the most important part of preventing corruption.
Anti-corruption measures in this area were set out by the government in the "Government's Anti-Corruption Strategy 2011-2012" which was updated for 2013 and 2014.
Some of these measures have been incorporated into the Public Procurement Act and subsequent implementing legislation.
The new legislation contains the following basic provisions: lowering the thresholds for small-scale contracts; the obligation of prior notice by the contracting authority, including the justification for the contract; the prohibition on limiting the number of tenderers in a restricted procedure and the abolition of the general possibility to limit the number of tenderers by drawing lots; obligation to publish contracts, including annexes; obligation to publish actual and lists of subcontractors.
Results of controls related to subsequent stages of procurement
Tender Documentation
Tender documents shall contain all information necessary to enable tenderers to prepare their tenders in an appropriate manner.
The documentation shall, in particular, allow for the processing of the offer price.
In order to do so, tenderers must not be exposed to risks they could not have anticipated.
The tender dossier shall contain, in particular, specifications, as well as requirements to prove the qualifications of the tenderers and criteria for selecting the most advantageous tender.
The Requirements of European Law and the Prevention of Corruption
In particular, European law contains requirements relating to technical specifications.
The specifications must limit competition as little as possible and all interested parties must have equal access to them.
Specifications must be neutral.
Requirements for specific specific products are permissible on a very limited scale in procurement documents.
The basic principle of preventing corruption is that the tender documents do not contain requirements that could restrict competition.
Furthermore, documentation should avoid ambiguities or inaccurate data that create scope for speculation and manipulation in the evaluation of tenders.
Provisions of German and Czech procurement law
Same or similar provisions
Both countries have transposed EU legal provisions into their national legislation.
The basic principle is that specifications should be given generally without reference to a specific product or brand.
The specifications must specify the deliveries and services required and detail the numbers of units of work and deliveries.
In normal cases, prices should be indicated by units of work or supply (e.g. in CZK/m or CZK/item).
It is permissible to define the work required in terms of functionality only in exceptional cases.
In principle, the rule is that specifications must be drawn up completely and unambiguously.
They must contain precise quantities and must not include works and supplies, the necessity of which is not obvious.
Reimbursements in the form of lump sums or according to time worked should be avoided where possible.
These parts of the tender dossier must exclude speculation and manipulation, based on ambiguous specifications.
The specifications must allow each bidder to prepare its bid with certainty and without excessive preparatory work.
The invitation to tender shall not be published until the tender dossier is complete.
Different provisions
In the Czech Republic there is no obligation for contracting authorities to award construction contracts in lots.
A procuring entity may divide a procurement into several separate lots if the nature of the subject of such a procurement so permits.
In Germany, the application of the rule of dividing works into parts is required, either quantitatively (sub-deliveries) or according to special considerations (specialised parts of the contract).
This allows SMEs to participate in the competition, increases the number of potential bidders and makes price fixing more difficult.
This type of procurement is usually considered the most appropriate in terms of providing value for money.
In the Czech Republic, tender documents must contain evaluation criteria in national and European procurement procedures.
In doing so, the tenderer should be comprehensively and exhaustively informed of the criteria by which its tender will be evaluated.
In Germany, the requirement to indicate the evaluation criteria in the notice applies only to European invitations to tender.
In national procurement procedures, evaluation criteria need only be included in the contract record, which is an internal administrative document.
When the tender documents are prepared by authorised independent experts, German law requires excluding from them any information that would assist in identifying the specific firms, architects or designers involved.
This is to prevent individual construction firms from obtaining, without the knowledge of the contracting authority, any additional information or other undue competitive advantages through contacts with these independent experts.
This is why independent experts are not allowed to publish documents relating to tenders and make them accessible for screening.
The contracting authority is the sole point of contact for the construction companies.
The Czech Republic does not currently have a similar provision.
In the case of major construction projects, there is almost always a need for additional works or modifications to existing works after the initial contract is concluded.
Legislation in both countries allows a multi-work contract to be awarded within a certain limit to the same contractor without the need to publish and conduct a new procurement procedure.
The volume of additional works under both European and German legislation may not exceed 50% of the original contract value.
In addition, additional works must be necessary and must be of a specific nature as part of the main contract.
In the Czech Republic, a stricter limit of 20% is applied to the award of additional works.
This may entail the risk of a greater time lag in the execution of the contract and the eventual incorporation of a new entity into an already running project.
Where a multi-work contract is awarded to the supplier of the original contract, German law contains a provision which prevents suppliers from arbitrarily increasing the price of modified works or multi-works.
Calculations must be made taking into account the prices agreed in the original contract.
In addition to multiple works, there may be inferiorities in construction, i.e. work that was not needed.
In the Czech Republic, inferiority and multiwork cannot be compensated for each other.
Main control findings
BHR
Technical specifications and product-neutral invitation to tender
In rare cases, specifications have referred to national technical requirements or standards, but these have in the meantime been replaced by EU standards.
In some cases, the specifications included data requiring a specific brand name, e.g. for a public sign resistance system, for preventing water ingress or for anti-noise cladding, although this was not necessary.
In some cases, the specifications were so detailed that only one product met the requirements.
Quantity specifications broken down into items
BRH did not detect, in a single case of checking procurement procedures, a non-conformity with the general requirements for the invitation to tender for supplies and services under the quantity specifications broken down into items.
Completeness of specifications
In most of the cases audited, tender documents were fully drawn up before the call for tenders was launched.
In various cases, however, documents had to be modified during the procurement process when changes in the items or timing had to be taken into account.
In rare cases, the tender documents included works and supplies whose need was uncertain.
One tender dossier for demolition work contained almost exclusively such items.
Division of contract and award criteria
In many cases, the required justification not to split deliveries into lots has not been demonstrated.
BRH did not find any shortcomings in the tender evaluation criteria prior to the award of the contract.
Include Names of Authorized Independent Experts
Many of the documents mentioned interested authorized experts.
Names were included on the front pages of the documents or on the plans or attached opinions.
In one case, the contracting authority even invited tenderers to contact an explicitly appointed architect with their questions on the procurement procedure.
For building projects, these cases were found mainly in tender documents, where self-employed experts were entrusted with drawing up the specifications.
The contracting authorities indicated that they did not have sufficient staffing capacity to scrutinise tender documents in detail.
OWN
Technical specifications and product-neutral invitation to tender
In the case of a public contract for the construction of a house with 12 apartments, the reference was directly to specific manufacturers and product brands.
The contracting authority did not admit the use of other similar types of products or justify why it required the supply of specific products.
In the case of the construction of a university facility, the tender documents contained references to specific commercial firms and specific product designations.
Overview of specifications and sub-specifications
In the case of the construction of certain industrial zones, the contracting authority did not clearly define in the tender documents the quantity and type of works, supplies or services required in the statement of assessment and established only the enclosure in cubic metres for each building as the unit of measurement.
Completeness of specifications
During the procurement procedure for the construction of the road circuit, the tender specifications for the restricted procedure were amended four times between August and September 2009.
This was due to changes in the project documentation due to an extension of the scope of the contract and a change in the technical conditions.
In the case of the construction of social objects, the tender documents had defects which forced the creation of multiple works (lack of precise focus, insufficient technical research in the construction, absence of drawings).
In the tender documents for the construction of the large library, the interior elements were listed only descriptively, with no specification of quality requirements for the products.
The supply of interior fittings was provided by subcontractors who purchased some premium and very expensive equipment.
Parts and contract award criteria
In the Czech Republic there is no obligation to award the contract in lots.
An analysis of three major roadworks under construction of the road circuit showed that when they were awarded in open procedure in lots (separately after selected objects), savings of up to CZK 790 million could be made from the achieved price of public contracts of CZK 15,508 million.
For example, by awarding six building sites as a separate part of the public contract, a saving of EUR 62.5 million could have been achieved.
A saving of CZK 287.8 million, i.e. also 25%, could be achieved by awarding 20 bridges as a separate part of the public contract.
The contracting authority had set qualification criteria in the tender documents for the construction of the roads, limiting the participation of small contractors in the tender procedure.
In the tender specifications for an open tender for a contractor for one construction of a motorway, the contracting authority requested, among other things, the contractor's practice of carrying out at least three roadworks with a financial volume of more than CZK 700 million each, completed or put into service over the last five years.
There are only nine firms in the Czech Republic that meet the condition of completing buildings over 700 million CZK in the last five years.
The open tender for the delivery of another motorway construction covered six separate works for which the contracting authority required a minimum annual volume of works of EUR 12 billion over the last three years.
Two-thirds of financial volume of road construction works (i.e. €2.5 billion out of €3.7 billion) in the period of 2008-2009 were realized by 5 suppliers or individually in form of consortium.
For 46 contracts for a total of 13.2 billion CZK, there were only two or even one bidder.
The SAO pointed out that limiting market potential can lead to large cost increases.
Multiwork Area
The contracting authority, when constructing the road circuit, did not, in two cases, conclude a written contract for the supply of the additional works and did not award the additional works by any of the procedures specified in the Act.
The value of pre-approved multi-work in one case was CZK 1,591.4 million excluding VAT (an increase of 21%) and in the other case CZK 4,135.8 million excluding VAT (an increase of 91%).
The reason for the extra work was mainly the ambiguous definition of the quantity and type of work required.
In the case of the construction of a university facility, the contracting authority did not include in the tender documents certain works and supplies of which it was aware at the time of the award of the contract that it needed to complete and validate the construction.
For those works and supplies, he concluded amendments to the works contract with the builder with an increase in price, in a negotiated procedure without publication, even though the volume of additional works exceeded the national limit of 20% of the value of the original contract.
In the context of the construction of the Justice Palace, the contracting authority reserved the right to award additional works without a procurement procedure.
During the construction, the contracting authority increased the scope of the contract by CZK 50.5 million.
Unit prices for additional works determined without competition were significantly higher than comparable works performed by the same contractor.
Other findings
The SAO found on a selected sample of buildings that the average construction cost of building one kilometre of a four-lane motorway in the Czech Republic is CZK 355.0 million.
According to long-term data obtained from the German Ministry of Construction, the average cost in Germany per kilometre of a four-lane motorway is CZK 260.0 million.
This comparison suggests that acquiring a motorway in the Czech Republic is more costly than in Germany.
Summary
The legal provisions relating to procurement documents are almost identical in both countries and comply with the requirements of European law.
The aim is to ensure full and equal competition and the prevention of corruption.
Specifications must be sufficiently precise to allow interested parties to calculate costs and prepare their bids.
Moreover, this requirement limits the scope for speculation and the contracting authority is protected against the need to commission additional works.
In Germany, the basic legal provisions on tender documents are supplemented by additional rules to further reduce the possibilities for restricting competition and for carrying out manipulations.
Although there are no detailed requirements of this kind in the Czech Republic, greater transparency is achieved through the publication of award criteria.
Specification requirements are mostly met in both countries.
In some cases, however, shortcomings have been identified that could lead to a reduction in competition.
The current rule in Germany requiring bulky construction projects to be subdivided into sections seems appropriate for fostering a competitive environment and achieving cost-effective results.
The SAO found that in the Czech Republic, awarding contracts for large-scale projects without piecing together or dividing into large-scale parts can limit the range of candidates to a small number of large suppliers.
The result of the strict requirement on bidder references applied in the Czech Republic for the selection of road works bidders is that public contracts are awarded only to a small number of bidders and thus small and medium-sized companies have no chance of competing.
The limited number of bidders also increases the risk of price fixing and other illegal arrangements.
The regulatory approaches in the two countries are different when it comes to awarding and paying for additional contracts for additional works or modified works.
If the value of the additional works under European and German law does not exceed 50 % of the value of the original contract, the additional works may be awarded without a procurement procedure and notice.
In the Czech Republic, a stricter limit of 20% applies.
THISsegmentISintentionallyLEFTblank
This limit is often exceeded by contracting authorities.
THISsegmentISintentionallyLEFTblank
If the 20 % limit is exceeded, additional works must be awarded following a new procurement procedure.
Dividing up the work into parts avoids both the reduction of competition and the consequent ill effects.
Determining the type of procurement procedure with respect to EU limits
By specifying the type of procurement procedure, the contracting authority defines the range of potential contractors that will be admitted to the competition.
A procuring entity may leave the scope of potential suppliers unlimited, or may limit the scope according to specified criteria.
A European procurement procedure must be conducted for major project works with a value that exceeds or exceeds the limits set by the EU.
The Requirements of European Law and the Prevention of Corruption
In choosing the type of procedure, the contracting authority must in particular comply with the requirements of European law relating to the competitive environment and transparency.
If the estimate of the total cost of the contract for the works required reaches or exceeds the current EU limit of €5.0 million ($125 million), an open or restricted procurement procedure at European level is always required.
In exceptional cases, a negotiated procedure or a competitive dialogue is permitted.
Where a construction project reaches the EU limit, the contracting authority may derogate to a limited extent from the European procurement procedure by launching a national procedure if the total value of the relevant part is below the threshold of €1.0 million ($25 million) and the total value of all parts does not exceed 20% of the estimated value of the entire contract.
Dividing a construction project or deliberately estimating its cost too low with the intention of avoiding a major obligation to launch a European procurement procedure is not permissible.
Choosing the type of governance is very important for preventing corruption, as reducing competition substantially increases the risk of manipulation.
Therefore, a detailed justification of the type of procedure chosen is an important part of the tender documentation, particularly in relation to the obligation to apply European procurement law.
Provisions of German and Czech procurement law
Same or similar provisions
Both countries have fully implemented EU legal requirements for procurement procedures and limits in their national legislation.
Different provisions
The Czech Republic has introduced the principle of equal validity of open and restricted procedures as well as for procurement procedures at national level.
Open and restricted procedures can always be chosen, while other types of procedure are only permitted if the conditions laid down by law are met.
Unlike the Czech regulation, the relevant German regulation of budgetary law and the principles of preventing corruption prefer open procedures or a public invitation to take part in the procedure.
Other types of proceedings, including restricted ones, are permitted only exceptionally, where the legislation so permits.
Such exemptions must be justified and documented.
The principle of open procedure preference is intended to ensure that restrictions on competition are prevented to the maximum extent and that the opportunity for manipulation (pre-selected specific candidates) is limited.
In Germany, this principle of public procurement is central to preventing corruption.
A correct estimate of the expected contract price is an important basis for deciding on the appropriate type of procurement procedure.
In estimating the costs of building projects, contracting authorities in Germany can rely on budgetary and cost databases of public administrations and other organisations.
These have been compiled on the basis of an analysis of completed construction projects and include, among other things, standard cost values for types of construction, construction units, building parts and sub-construction works.
On the basis of such standard cost values, it is possible to estimate the contract value of the planned project or works.
Only a few building administrations in Germany already have a similar database for road construction.
However, the database is expected to be expanded nationally.
Contracting authorities in the Czech Republic do not use any central database or average prices of comparable objects to determine the expected costs of land-based contracts.
In many cases, the determination of costs is based on an estimate by the entity financing the project or based on the value of the resources available to that entity.
Measures for standardisation are still at an early stage.
Under the anti-corruption programme for the period 2011 to 2012, the relevant ministries are to draw up a database of customary prices for standardised commodities.
The use of a price system to determine the expected price of a contract is determined by a new regulation requiring the subject of the contract to be set out in detail.
The pricing system should serve as a set of information on construction and assembly works, materials and products and other data to calculate the necessary costs and to establish unit prices.
The Ministry of Transport has issued normative prices for transport works for inspection, which are regularly updated.
The database is designed to price the project (investment) plan and should play a comparative function in the subsequent stages of the preparation of the works.
Main BRH control findings
Tender Selection
The audited contracting authorities generally awarded contracts through an open procedure or a public call for tenders and thus complied with the requirements of budget and procurement legislation.
In 2011, the share of tenders in the total volume of federal building projects in the field of building construction was 59.5%.
In road construction, the share was 94.8%.
BRH found that in some cases the use of in-camera competition was not adequately justified.
In the case of blanket control, the BRH found, based on information collected from more than 16,000 procedures, that restricted management has a clear disadvantage compared to public management and public challenge, and restricted procedures also increase the risk of manipulation and corruption.
THISsegmentISintentionallyLEFTblank
According to the BRH study, bidding prices in the restricted procedure and negotiated procedure were 6% to 10% higher than in public procedures.
Parallel studies by the Ministry of Construction and the Land Audit Court confirmed the differences in value for money obtained in public and restricted procedures.
Indeed, a study by the Ministry of Construction found that prices in restricted procedures were twice as high as those in public procedures.
Compliance with EU limits
For projects estimated to have a total contract value of more than €5 million ($125 million), the audited contracting authorities generally prepared procurement plans indicating which works would be awarded in the European tender and which in the national tender.
According to the tendering plans, the proportion of works commissioned under European rules was always more than 80% of the total.
There has not been a case of circumvention of the requirement for European governance in the form of an exorbitantly low cost estimation.
However, in some cases the minimum proportion for pan-European management has not been reached due to a calculation error.
In the course of carrying out the works, some contracting authorities have unlawfully changed the contracting plan.
They did not provide any justification for these changes and did not demonstrate that the minimum proportion of European governance would subsequently be respected.
The share of European tenders by project accounts was generally lower than the share provided for in the procurement plan.
If the expected contract values for construction projects exceeded the European limits, contracting authorities did not apply the European procedure to all individual sub-lots exceeding €1m (£25m).
Instead, they awarded the relevant contracts on the basis of a national procedure, without giving good reasons for the need to deviate from the requirement.
OWN
Choose tender type
In the case of several large construction projects (e.g. construction of a road section, facilities for a major sporting event and construction of apartments), the contracting authority divided one project into several lots, although these sub-contracts were of the same or similar nature.
The division reduced the expected value of the contract and the contracts were awarded under a less demanding regime or even outside the statutory regime.
Methods for calculating the forecast price
The pricing standards applicable to road construction projects have not yet been an effective tool for setting the expected price.
They were based on prices quoted in the completed proceedings, which were however burdened by both uneconomic and excessive profits for suppliers.
The SAO found that actual prices for three parts of the road circuit were about 37% lower than these price norms.
The Ministry of Transport is to update the standards in relation not only to prices from the past, but also to other comparable markets and price developments.
In the case of ground-based construction, the estimated price was based on the budget of the project carried out by the architects or the investor's estimate.
The actual price then differed considerably from the forecast price.
The projected cost of building a large library was twice as high as the contract price, but it increased by CZK 54 million, or 27%, during the execution.
Similarly, for the construction of a depository for this library, the contract price was 33% lower than forecast.
In other cases, the successful applicant's price was higher than the anticipated price and increased even further in the course of implementation.
For the construction of the large library, it was by 400 million CZK, or 22%. For the construction of the Justice Palace, the actual price increased disproportionately (by 139%) over the increase in functional areas (only by 12%) during the construction process.
Summary
Both countries have implemented European provisions on choosing the type of procurement procedure and respecting European limits in their national legislation, so the respective national arrangements of the two countries are almost identical.
Nevertheless, the principle of public governance in Germany is stricter than European and current national regulations in the Czech Republic.
Compared to a narrower procurement process, a public procedure or a public invitation to participate gives more competitors the opportunity to participate in the procurement process.
These types of procurement generate a large number of bids that guarantee the best value for money effect.
Prevention of corruption is significantly strengthened as the pool of candidates is not constrained by decisions taken in advance by the contracting authority, thereby allowing room for price manipulation and fixation among bidders.
The studies confirmed the advantages of the German provision on preference for open procedures or public calls for participation.
Czech law allows contracting authorities to award contracts in an open or restricted procedure.
However, the latest legislation in the Public Procurement Act introduces a ban on contracting authorities limiting the number of tenderers in restricted procedures.
Closer management has now come very close to open management.
In Germany, contracting authorities are largely fulfilling the requirement of a preference for an open procedure or a public call for competition, while in the Czech Republic some projects have been subdivided in violation of the law, in order to circumvent the requirement to choose the relevant type of procurement procedure.
However, in the case of some projects carried out in Germany, European limits were not respected, which resulted in the minimum proportion not being reached in Europe-wide procedures.
A satisfactory system has not yet been put in place in the Czech Republic to facilitate the correct calculation of the expected costs of construction works and projects.
Where appropriate, contracting entities should ensure, through more extensive controls, that the requirements for the choice of the type of procurement process and the volume of contracts awarded in European procedures are met, or create the conditions necessary to allow a correct calculation of the expected costs.
Provisional information and notice of initiation
In cases where contracts are awarded under the European procedure, the contracting authority must publish a procurement report.
Preliminary information and contract notices are an important basis for European competition.
The Requirements of European Law and the Prevention of Corruption
By means of prior information notices, the contracting authority must indicate the basic specifications of the projects envisaged.
Preliminary notices are required for projects where the total cost estimate is equal to or greater than the European limit and where the contracting authority wishes to reduce the time limit for the submission of tenders in individual procurement procedures.
A procuring entity is required to publish a procurement notice with detailed information for all procurement that requires a European procedure.
The information shall be binding on the sponsor upon publication.
Among other things, the notice shall contain specifications, time limits for the procedure and for the completion of the contract, specific requirements and selection criteria and their weightings.
A common procurement vocabulary must be used to describe the subject of the contract.
Preliminary information and contract notices are published in the EU in the Official Journal of the EU and on the profile of the contracting authority.
To avoid discrimination against foreign candidates, national notices should not contain any additional information and should not be published before the text of the notice is sent to the EU Publications Office.
Incomplete or inaccurate information in advance notifications can distort competition or even ease manipulation.
Provisions of German and Czech procurement law
Same or similar provisions
Both countries have fully implemented EU pre-information and tender notices provisions in their national legislation.
Different provisions
In the Czech Republic, prior information and notices must also be published for contracts for which a European procedure is not required.
Procurement procedures shall not begin earlier than one month after dispatch of the prior information notice.
It is only in exceptional cases that a provisional disclosure can be waived.
In Germany, prior information is not required in cases where a European procedure is not necessary.
In the case of national procedures, the public invitation must be published on the federal government's website and, where appropriate, in the press.
In restricted procedures without competition from participants, a notice must be published if the estimated contract price is equal to or above €25,000 ($625,000).
Preliminary notices must explain all planned parts of the project.
This is an important requirement given the number of small contracts in Germany.
Division of projects into smaller parts is not as common in the Czech Republic and this measure is considered less important.
Main control findings
BRH
Advance notice
The contracting authorities published advance notices for the projects, which included works to be awarded in a European procedure.
In some open procedures, they shortened the deadline, although the number of days between the sending of the advance notice and the sending of the contract notice was less than 52 days.
In the case of other abridged procedures, the annual time limits for preliminary information had expired and were to be renewed.
In many cases, the advance notice did not indicate an anticipated split of the construction works into multiple lots.
Tender Notice
Some of the notices contained codes from the Common Dictionary that deviated from the content of the advance notice or did not specify precisely the work to be performed.
In isolated cases, notifications at national level included more information, e.g. on performance dates, or were published before a notice was published in the EU Official Journal.
Ex-ante transparency
One contracting authority hired an outside company to publish a national notice.
It published notices on its platform, the use of which was subject to a fee.
But the contracting authority did not check whether that company had to comply with the requirement to send further notices to a free online government portal.
The sample found that only a third of notifications were actually published on the government portal.
OWN
Tender Notice
In the construction of the wastewater treatment plant, the contracting authority did not lay down in the notice of a restricted tendering procedure for the works contractor the criteria by which it would reduce a large number of tenders for the evaluation procedure.
The method of selecting (reducing) tenders for evaluation has not been documented by the contracting authority.
Previous legislation allowed for a reduction in the number of bids.
When constructing the road circuit, the contracting authority did not specify in the notice of the opening of the restricted procedure or in the qualification documents the values by which it would assess the level of compliance with the qualification criteria or the number of tenderers to be invited.
The contracting authority assessed the qualification as having been met by all six candidates under the restricted procedure, but excluded one of them as having met in the last sixth place.
