The contracting authority's procedure was not transparent as it did not publish in advance information on what qualification data would be evaluated and how.
Publishing calls
For construction works in former military tours, the procuring entity failed to publish a written invitation to tender, in violation of the law.
Conclusions and recommendations/summary
The two-step information process with prior information and the publication of the Notice of Initiation is an important tool for meeting the transparency requirements and strengthening the competitive environment applied in European proceedings.
By extending the obligation to publish preliminary information in the Czech Republic also for national procurement procedures and strengthening this obligation in Germany for restricted procedures, both countries have increased the transparency of procurement procedures and improved the competitive environment.
Selection of candidates in restricted procurement procedures
Contrary to an open procedure, restricted procurement procedures do not allow participation by all potential bidders who would be interested.
In restricted procedures, the procuring entity first selects potential suppliers who will be asked to submit a tender.
The Requirements of European Law and the Prevention of Corruption
EU provisions allow a cap on the number of candidates in European restricted procedures.
However, a restricted procedure requires at least five bidders to be invited to submit a bid and, in the case of a negotiated procedure with publication, at least three potential bidders must be invited.
In any event, the number of candidates invited must be sufficiently broad to guarantee genuine competition.
The selection criteria shall be objective and non-discriminatory and published in advance.
The selection of potential candidates to be invited is highly susceptible to manipulation, e.g. the preferences of some of them.
Reducing competition to a few bidders may ease their collusion.
This has a negative impact on the outcome of the proceedings.
Therefore, the contracting authority must take measures to prevent tampering and ensure that it has effective control so that the procedure is transparent and credible.
Provisions of German and Czech procurement law
Same or similar provisions
The provisions for selecting candidates are similar in both countries.
In addition to the requirements of the European directive, contracting entities should rotate tenderers invited to submit a tender.
Nor is it permissible to restrict competition from a regional point of view.
Contracting entities must document and justify the selection of candidates and the selection process.
Different provisions
In Germany, it is required to invite at least three candidates in a restricted procedure and several suitable candidates in a negotiated procedure.
Except in two cases, the new Czech regulations do not permit limiting the number of candidates approached in the event of a restricted and negotiated procedure.
In Germany, when selecting candidates, the ‘four eyes principle’ must be respected.
While one worker proposes their list, another must substantially modify the proposal, e.g. by deleting a proposed candidate or installing another.
This is to avoid a situation where an individual can definitively determine the range of candidates who will take part in the procedure.
In the absence of such a provision, far-reaching illegal collusion between the parties is allowed.
The list of tenderers invited to submit a tender must be confidential and may not be divulged.
Authorised independent experts are also obliged to do so.
There is no similar measure in the Czech Republic, nor any measure for confidential treatment of the list of candidates.
Under German provisions, they may not designate candidates from outside companies, but may submit proposals to the contracting authority.
There is no similar requirement in the Czech Republic.
Main control findings
BRH
Number of Leads
Except in a few cases, the requirement to reach a minimum number of candidates was met.
An analysis of almost 7,000 works contracts with a value up to the national limit showed that, for restricted procedures, contracting authorities invited an average of 6.6 potential bidders and, for direct negotiated procedures, an average of 4.4 bidders.
Change of suppliers and regional restrictions
In particular, the contracting authorities did not change the range of applicants contacted in the area of building construction because they had a good experience with those with whom they had worked so far.
THISsegmentISintentionallyLEFTblank
The contracting authorities argued that, in the event of a non-public procedure, they should guarantee to reach out to candidates whose suitability they had already ascertained beforehand.
Although there was no evidence for illegal regional restrictions, the pool of non-public bidders included a larger proportion of regional bidders than it did for public procedures.
The contracting authorities explained this by the low level of the contract compared to the public tender and the high cost of travel for potential bidders.
Cross-checking principle and proposals from self-employed professionals
For the most part, contracting authorities ignored the obligation to amend the list of candidates proposed by self-employed professionals.
In particular, they argued lack of staff, time pressure or inadequate knowledge of a suitable candidate.
In many cases, the records did not show who proposed the candidate who was asked to participate in the procedure.
A check of some samples showed that the list of potential candidates was drawn up by self-employed experts and accepted by the contracting authority without changes.
As a result, contracting authorities were insufficiently protective against potential tampering.
Confidentiality of treatment of potential candidates lists
One contracting authority kept a list of candidates for the invitation to tender in a database to which all staff had access at any time.
This allowed for manipulation or unauthorised disclosure of the names of prospective or admitted bidders, who could then agree on a price.
Documentation
The dossier gave reasons for the selection of candidates in only a few cases.
OWN
Selection criteria for candidates
When the road circuit was built, the bidders were not informed of what of their qualification data would be evaluated and how.
For the event related to the development and modernisation of waterways and ports in the tender notice for participation in the restricted procedure, the criteria for limiting the number of bidders were not established.
All eight tenderers met the qualifications but this exceeded the number to which the contracting authority had limited participation in the procurement procedure.
The Commission then selected the candidates using a random selection method — by drawing lots, which was legal at the time.
That procedure should already have been mentioned as a criterion in the tender notice.
Summary
In view of the high risk of manipulation in selecting the winning bidder in limited procurement procedures, both countries have put in place special measures to prevent illicit restrictions on competition.
The German rules limit such opportunities for manipulation by means of so-called cross-checking (the four eyes principle), by keeping the list of tenderers confidential and by prohibiting external companies from making proposals for the selection of tenderers.
The Czech rules aim to prevent potential restrictions to manipulation mainly by an unlimited number of bidders.
However, the inspection found that some of the said rules were not applied to a sufficient extent and that an inadmissible restriction on the pool of candidates was not prevented in some cases.
Contracting authorities are obliged to eliminate the aforementioned shortcomings completely.
Submission of tenders
The procurement stage, in which potential tenderers can prepare their bids on the basis of the tender documents they have received, begins with the publication of the tender information and/or the call for tenders.
Until the opening date of the envelopes, potential tenderers may submit their tenders to the contracting authority for the award of the contract.
The Requirements of European Law and the Prevention of Corruption
EU law does not prescribe specific requirements at this stage of the procurement procedure.
The contracting authority must follow the national rules governing this stage of the procedure and prevent potential manipulation.
Those rules relate in particular to organisation and control.
Provisions of German and Czech procurement law
Same or similar provisions
The principle is that tender documents on the basis of which potential contractors prepare their tenders must always be sent by the contracting authority.
Projection offices, which are involved in management, should not fulfil this role.
The contracting authority must ensure that all candidates have received the same information during this stage of the procurement procedure.
In the event of confusion in the procurement documents or where further questions arise, only the contracting authority may supply the information to the tenderers.
The design offices in charge of drafting the tender dossier cannot provide any information.
Throughout this stage, any contact between the design office and the tenderers must be prevented.
They must remain anonymous throughout, as allowing them to contact them may adversely affect the competition.
Tenders sent by post or submitted directly must be submitted in a sealed envelope.
Electronically submitted bids must be encoded.
Different provisions
In Germany, the contracting authority may instruct insured contractors to print and distribute the documentation, and their employees must be bound to observe confidentiality.
In Germany, tenders received must be marked with the date of receipt and sent directly and immediately in their original state (sealed) to the official in charge of their safekeeping.
This staff member shall have no involvement in the procurement process.
Tenders received must be locked until the opening date of the envelopes.
In the Czech Republic, offers received are marked with a serial number.
The contracting authority shall draw up a report with the details of the competition, including the number of the tender and information on the preparation and receipt of the tender.
There are no special rules for the safekeeping of bids.
Main control findings
BRH
BRH checked the assignment of the administration building.
Submission of tender documents
The audited contracting authorities circulated the tender documents either directly or entrusted an external firm to print and distribute them.
Self-employed people did not send any documentation.
Information to be provided to candidates
For the part of the procurement documents relating to interviewing and obtaining information, the procuring entity did not follow the rule of separate planning from the procurement procedure.
Contrary to the relevant regulations, he often did not include in the documentation the names of his contact staff, but rather the staff of the Project Management Unit.
Some tender notices even included the names of firms or self-employed persons involved in the preparation of tender documents.
In rare cases, candidates who raised questions during the procurement process did not receive identical information.
Menu Labelling
For the contracting authorities, all tenders received were stamped and receipted.
However, in some cases in the negotiated procedure, offers were accepted by self-employed professionals, in breach of the one-building rule against corruption.
Custody of the tenders
Once marked, not all tenders were passed on directly and without delay to the official assigned to keep them in custody locked the tenders in a steel cabinet.
For one contracting authority, tenders were temporarily placed in an open mailroom compartment that all employees had access to.
Another contracting authority placed a mailbox in a corridor without constant supervision, and some of the bids thrown in the mailbox stayed there overnight.
The box opening was wide and unsecured, making it accessible not only to employees.
OWN
The SAO did not find any shortcomings in this area.
Summary
Rules are laid down in both countries to prevent contacts, in particular between interested companies and design offices cooperating in the procurement process, throughout the process.
Equal treatment is ensured by sending the same information to all candidates.
Rules to prevent manipulation and corruption are appropriate for the receipt, custody and confidentiality of offers.
In Germany the requirements are somewhat more elaborated than in the Czech Republic.
In Germany, accumulated inspection records have shown breaches of the rules in some areas.
These discrepancies can easily be corrected.
Opening envelopes
Opening envelopes
During the opening of the envelopes, tenders that have so far been closed are opened, their contents read and published.
The period for which a tenderer is bound by its tender shall begin when the closing date for the submission of envelopes expires or when they are opened and shall end on a date determined by the contracting authority.
The Requirements of European Law and the Prevention of Corruption
European legislation does not contain any specific provisions dealing with the opening of envelopes.
However, national laws and regulations contain extensive requirements to protect both tenders and competition during this sensitive phase of the entire procurement process.
The principles of ‘more eyes’, transparency and trustworthiness in the process of opening envelopes are intended to prevent the manipulation and editing of tenders already submitted.
Provisions of German and Czech procurement law
Same or similar provisions
Opening of envelopes must be carried out by several persons appointed by the contracting authority.
Conformity with the ‘more eyes’ principle is intended to prevent individuals from covertly manipulating offers.
Candidates may be present when the envelopes are opened.
At the time of opening, all envelopes must be placed before the chairman of the commission and opened one by one.
Tenders received late must be disregarded.
To ensure transparency in the opening of envelopes, this phase, including the conclusions (results), must be recorded in writing. The record must include, but is not limited to, the names of the bidders and the prizes offered.
The entry must be read and signed by the group leader.
The minutes may also be signed by the candidates present.
Different provisions
In the Czech Republic, a commission is being set up to open envelopes.
The participation of independent experts is permitted.
Panel members may also be tasked with deliberating at subsequent stages of the procedure, i.e. evaluating tenders.
In Germany, the opening of envelopes takes place in the presence of the manager and the registrar.
The manager must be an employee of the contracting authority.
The head and, in the case of building, the recorder shall not be involved in the preparatory stage (drafting of the procurement documents), shall not be allowed to decide on the award of the contract and may not be a member in the subsequent processing (management) of the contract.
German regulations require that the opening minutes contain not only the offer prices, but also all information about discounts and special (unusual) symptoms, such as damaged envelopes when tenders are received or received late.
In the Czech Republic, in addition to the tender prices, an indication of the language of the tender and the signature authority of the tenderer must be included in the minutes drawn up.
German regulations require all parts of opened envelopes to be marked in order to avoid subsequent changes.
Tenders, usually marked in the form of a date-stamped perforation, must be kept confidential (secret) after the envelopes have been opened and may be disclosed only to persons who further process them.
There are no similar measures in the Czech Republic.
If a single bid is received in the Czech Republic, the envelope must not be opened and the tenderer must be notified immediately.
In Germany, they do not have this arrangement, and if a single tender is accepted, it is opened and evaluated.
Main control findings
BRH
Cross-checking principle
During the opening of the envelopes, the contracting authorities adhered to the four-eyes principle.
There was always a supervisor and a recorder present who was not assigned a different role.
However, in rare cases, for small negotiated procedures, individuals have opened tenders sent to them, without the involvement of the contracting authority.
Envelope Opening Progress
records from opening envelopes did not always document the process.
In some cases, tenders submitted late were not marked and in some cases signatures were missing.
Tagging incoming bids
The contracting authorities marked bids with a date perforation, which was in line with the requirements.
Some of them have labelled offers incorrectly in the past, but changed their practice after notifying BRH.
THISsegmentISintentionallyLEFTblank
OWN
Deadline for receipt of tenders
In road repairs, for one event, a bidder's bid was submitted to the contracting authority one month late, which nevertheless accepted it.
Summary
The essential parts of the opening of envelopes in the two countries are identical with regard to the principle of "more eyes" and the principle of transparency, and thus allow the risk of corruption and manipulation to be substantially reduced.
The German rules are more complex than the Czech Republic, especially the requirements for precise documentation of the procedure.
Marking, safe storage and respect for the confidentiality of tenders substantially limit the possibility of subsequent amendments to tenders submitted.
In addition, the personnel responsible for opening the envelopes may not carry out any other activities related to the contract to be awarded.
The findings, despite the discovery of some irregularities, confirm that the rules on opening envelopes are largely applied.
Assessment and evaluation of bids
The awarding authority must examine and evaluate tenders received within the time limit set.
At the same time, it must award the contract to the tenderer who best meets the award criteria in the tender.
The Requirements of European Law and the Prevention of Corruption
EU law sets out the process for assessing and evaluating offers in several steps:
The suitability of candidates is examined as a first step.
They must have a good economic and financial position and the required professional and technical knowledge and skills.
Candidates who have ever been convicted of bribery or defrauding must always be excluded from procurement procedures.
Tenders of non-eliminated tenderers must be evaluated in terms of numerical accuracy, technical suitability and value for money on the basis of previously published tender criteria.
The contracting authority must identify the most economically advantageous tender and, in particular, examine all significantly low price offers.
Review and evaluation is particularly sensitive and susceptible to manipulation.
For example, tenderers may be excluded without a legitimate reason, or tenders that do not meet the requirements may be left to compete.
The result of the evaluation may be wrongly influenced by the preference given to a tenderer or its disadvantage, thereby preventing the selection of the most economically advantageous tender.
The contracting authority must therefore take special measures in selecting the persons involved in the process and must ensure that they meet the requirements of good repute and technical knowledge.
They also need to make sure that the process and the decisions taken are transparent and credible.
Provisions of German and Czech procurement law
Same or similar provisions
Consistent with EU requirements, the relevant provisions in both countries require the examination and evaluation of tenders in successive steps.
During the evaluation, the requirements relating to the suitability of candidates, the evaluation criteria and their weightings set out in the notice or tender dossier may not be altered.
The criteria established to assess the suitability of candidates cannot be applied further.
Further evaluation may only be carried out on the basis of evaluation criteria.
The tenders submitted must first be examined from a formal point of view, whether they are complete or do not include suspicious elements likely to encourage manipulation.
Where tenderers are excluded from further examination and evaluation of their tenders, they must be informed immediately.
If, as a result of the evaluation of the tenders, a disproportionately low tender price is found, the tenderer concerned must be given the opportunity to comment before any elimination of his tender.
The reasons for the decision must be transparently documented.
Different provisions
In Germany, bidders must observe confidentiality and only authorised persons may take part in the evaluation of bids.
The mathematical accuracy of tenders can only be reviewed by the contracting authority.
Only staff who are not integrated into the decision-making phase of the selection of the contractor or the implementation phase of the project may be entrusted with the evaluation.
There are no precise provisions on who can carry out further reviews and evaluations.
If external companies are entrusted with reviewing and evaluating the bids, they must be aware that the bids are confidential and honourably respect that fact.
They may only make proposals for decisions to the contracting authority.
In the Czech Republic, tenders must be examined and evaluated by a commission, appointed by the contracting authority, which will prepare a report proposing the conclusion of a contract.
However, in both countries, the final decision depends on the contracting authority.
If, in Germany, the documents and declarations required in connection with the submission of tenders are incomplete, the tenderers concerned must subsequently produce the required documents within six days.
THISsegmentISintentionallyLEFTblank
If bidders fail to meet this obligation, their bids must be eliminated.
In the Czech Republic, the contracting authority may decide to give the tenderer the opportunity to complete the missing documents.
If a candidate fails to substantiate them, they must be eliminated.
In Germany, not only the overall price but also the unit prices have to be reviewed.
If the price structure of the bid indicates that prices for parts of the overall project have been estimated disproportionately high or low, bidders must be asked to explain.
If they do not provide an explanation and if the contracting authority can prove that the tender contains compensatory prices, such tenders must be excluded.
This is to prevent bidders from submitting price calculations on the assumption that substantial quantitative changes will be made to the different parts of the overall project, which may lead to a significant increase in project costs.
In the Czech Republic, the overall price of a work is evaluated and the prices of the parts are not examined in detail.
Price compensation is not a reason to exclude a bid.
In Germany, a contract cannot be awarded to a tenderer who offers a disproportionately high or low price.
Where one or more tenders differ in price by 10% or more than the price estimated by the contracting authority or other tenders, tenderers must demonstrate that their tender has been credibly and convincingly aggregated.
If they are unable to do so, the contracting authority should exclude them.
In the Czech Republic, a tender may be cancelled after a price review if only tenders with unreasonably low prices have been received and the tenderers have not justified the prices.
If the price of the tender is significantly higher than the contracting authority's estimate, this is not a reason for refusing to award the contract to the tenderer concerned (unlike under German procurement law).
If there is any suspicion in Germany of conduct intended to restrict competition, the contracting authority is obliged to report this fact to the relevant superior authority without delay.
The latter must decide what action to take and whether it is appropriate to inform the antitrust or law enforcement authorities.
Furthermore, they must approve (e.g. when the contract exceeds €50,000 and the contract has not been awarded to the lowest bidder) whether the contract will be awarded when the contract is negotiated or whether the procurement procedure will be cancelled.
Czech rules do not regulate this area.
Main control findings
BRH
Suitability testing and procurement criteria
Most of the procurement procedures examined complied with the relevant requirements.
For some of the recommendations of potential contractors, made by self-employed persons, the contracting authorities themselves did not check the suitability of the tenderers.
THISsegmentISintentionallyLEFTblank
In some procurement cases, insufficient documentation was provided as to whether all published evaluation criteria were taken into account by the contracting authority in the choice, or whether the selection decision was based solely on the price criterion.
Selection of interested parties and confidentiality of offers
Some contracting authorities used the services of unauthorised persons, such as self-employed persons or employees of building authorities.
They were integrated into the planning or implementation process of the construction projects, performing functions that were to be held solely by the contracting authority.
The contracting authorities sought to substantiate this with their insufficient human resources in the sector.
In some cases, they used self-employed professionals to review offers of services by persons who were not formally bound to maintain the confidentiality of offers and to carry out with due care their obligations under the contract.
As far as contracting authorities were concerned, there was generally no conclusive record of whether offers in the safe-keeping of self-employed professionals were available only to specified persons.
Menu review and mathematical review
Some contracting authorities passed open envelopes for further examination to self-employed professionals without first undertaking the prescribed examination of tenders and the mathematical examination and without documenting the results of those examinations.
In the event of any intention to manipulate, third parties may have made changes to tenders which could not be identified by the contracting authority.
Examination of the documents revealed a number of cases where unusual elements in the offers were not adequately labelled or where data on prices and products were missing.
Performance audits
A sample check by one contracting authority revealed that in 75% of procurement cases, the most advantageous tenders deviated by more than 10% from the pre-estimated costs.
One out of 20 tenders was cancelled due to inadequate tender prices.
In many cases, there was no record or sufficient documentation of the examination of tenders or of negotiations with tenderers for further clarification.
In many cases, detailed information such as unit prices of the most important invoiced items was also missing.
Changes in market prices or increases in material costs were often cited as the reason for deviations.
The contracting authorities argued that it was difficult to identify and prove balancing prices.
In the case of one call for tenders, made by an entity outside the range of ordinary contracting entities, a 66% lower price was offered compared to the initial cost estimate.
No data were recorded on closer examination of cost or on the reasons for deviations.
In clarifying this question, the contracting authority found no indication of price dumping on the tenderer to which the contract would eventually be awarded.
It was therefore not made clear whether the initial cost estimate was excessive or whether the price offered was too low.
Later, the contracting authority rated that tenderer as a "low-cost company" that was unable to meet either quality requirements or deadlines.
In another procurement procedure, the contracting authority awarded the contract to a tenderer whose price was 35% higher than the initial cost estimate.
The contracting authority did not annul the procurement procedure and put forward an unfounded argument that the said tenderer could be presumed not to have based its pricing on unreasonably high costs.
Suspected irregularities
Two cases of irregularities were found in one contracting authority, and these were reported to the firm responsible for technical supervision.
In one procurement, two bidders submitted bids containing identical price calculations, prompting the contracting authority to exclude both bids.
The inspections did not reveal any significant shortcomings in the application of the other provisions.
OWN
Suitability test
For many building events, such as university buildings, roads and railways, contracting authorities did not exclude tenderers from participating in a procurement procedure, even though they did not meet the requirements of the contracting authority as set out in the tender specifications, or did not provide evidence of qualification to the extent required, or, conversely, one contracting authority could not substantiate or explain the reason for the exclusion of a candidate who met the requirements.
Rating
In the case of the construction of a sewage treatment plant, although the various evaluation criteria of the contract award procedure for the contractor were established, they were not respected by the contracting authority during the procedure.
For the railway infrastructure development action, the weightings of the award evaluation criteria were not set at the start of the evaluation process, but only at the beginning of the evaluation.
For the road repair project, the assessment of the criterion of ‘shortening the construction period’ was not carried out correctly.
For second- and third-place bidders, the evaluation incorrectly indicated a greater reduction in construction time than indicated in their bids.
Documentation of candidate selection reasons
For the three housing promotion actions, the contracting authority did not substantiate on what criteria it assessed the bids received and how it selected the most appropriate of them.
The accuracy of the selection of small-scale contractors associated with the construction of a road circuit with an estimated price of up to 500K.
The CZK could not be verified, as suppliers were selected only on the basis of telephone consultations on price and delivery date, but no proof of these negotiations was produced.
Summary
In both countries, the rules for the verification and evaluation of tenders comply with EU requirements.
They differ, however, with regard to the specific steps of the procedure and the choice of the individuals involved in the procedure.
Unlike the Czech Republic, in Germany these steps of the procurement process are governed by stricter and more detailed requirements, especially as regards the application of the cross-principle and the protection of tenders.
The same applies to the rules for verifying the appropriateness of the prices offered.
In both countries, it was found in some cases of checks that substantial steps in the process of verifying and evaluating tenders had not been documented at all and, if so, only partially.
The lack of control tools and transparency in decision-making during the review and evaluation of tenders allows for manipulation.
The inspections showed serious deficiencies, such as the transmission of tenders to third parties without prior proper examination and documentation of tenders by the contracting authority.
Where contracting entities entrust a third party with the performance of the functions related to the examination and evaluation of tenders, appropriate measures and increased scrutiny of measures against increased possibility of manipulation are necessary.
In checking the legitimacy of the prices, the audited German contracting authorities did not in all cases make use of the complete set of tools available and did not address their own pricing structure.
In the Czech Republic, weaknesses were found in the evaluation of the suitability of offers and the use of evaluation criteria.
Completion of tender
After the tenders have been examined and evaluated, the procurement process is concluded with the award of the contract or the cancellation of the procedure.
The Requirements of European Law and the Prevention of Corruption
In the case of a European procedure, all tenderers must be informed in advance of the decision to award the contract.
Suppliers shall have an opportunity to review the decision taken within a limited period of 10 or 15 days and to request a review if appropriate.
THISsegmentISintentionallyLEFTblank
The 10-day time limit applies to electronic submissions and 15 days to other submissions.
The contract may not be concluded before the expiry of this period or before the review procedure has been decided.
If the review body rejects the complaint and upholds the decision, candidates and tenderers must be given the opportunity to have the correctness of the application of the procurement law reviewed by an independent court.
This measure ensures that no contracting authority will be able to circumvent the legal protection of contractors by swiftly implementing the award decision.
The contracting authority must document the progress of the procedure appropriately.
Provisions of German and Czech procurement law
Same or similar provisions
Both countries have implemented management information obligations.
In both countries, the final decision on the outcome of the procurement procedure rests with the contracting authority.
However, it cannot arbitrarily deviate from the results of a properly conducted screening and evaluation.
The contracting authority is to conclude the contract at the specified time.
This is because after this deadline the bidder is not bound by its bid.
The contract content must not deviate from the winning bid.
Unsuccessful tenderers are to be informed of the result in good time, stating the reason why their tender has not been accepted.
Cancellation of the procurement procedure is permissible only if certain conditions are met, e.g. if the prices in the tenders do not comply with the specifications.
Different provisions
In the Czech Republic, the mandatory waiting time before the conclusion of a contract of 10 or 15 days applies to both European and national procedures.
In Germany, the equivalent provision applies only to European proceedings.
In all other procedures in Germany, tenderers cannot be informed of the award of the contract before a decision is taken on its EU provisions and review award.
In the Czech Republic, the tender procedure must be cancelled if a single tender was received or only one tender met the requirements for its consideration and evaluation.
In Germany, a contract must be awarded even if a single tender has been received, provided that the tender meets the conditions set out in the tender specifications and is cost-effective.
Main control findings
BRH
The inspections found few inconsistencies in the completion of the audited procurement processes.
In some instances of European proceedings, the enabling companies received letters before the reporting deadline, which may invalidate the contract.
Also, in several cases of open procedures, letters of authorisation contained modifications incompatible with the tender content, e.g. new deadlines.
In some cases, contracting authorities did not meet the award deadlines.
In one open procedure, the contracting authority selected the second most advantageous tender because a tenderer offering a price more than 50% lower no longer wished to perform the contract at the price originally offered.
OWN
In the refurbishment and construction of the college, in one case the contracting authority did not conclude a works contract with the contractor in accordance with the draft contract contained in the tender of the successful tenderer.
For some road repair and maintenance operations, the construction of the motorway and the ring road, contracts were concluded later than the statute.
For construction works in former military tours, the contracting authority sent a notice of selection of the most suitable tender to all tenderers after the statutory deadline.
Summary
Both countries have implemented EU rules on the termination of procurement procedures.
Legal protection for failed candidates contributes to curbing corruption.
The Czech Public Procurement Law includes the Institute of Legal Protection even in cases of contracts below EU limits.
In both countries, it was found that the limited time limit for concluding a contract was exceeded.
This can cause adverse economic consequences and increase the risk of manipulation.
In the future, contracting authorities should eliminate such shortcomings when concluding procurement procedures.
Documentation, notifications, information obligations
The procurement process, with all its stages, relevant findings and decisions, including relevant justifications, shall be documented in a transparent manner.
The completion of the procurement process at European level must be published.
The data on procurement procedures are summarised in EU statistics.
The Requirements of European Law and the Prevention of Corruption
The obligation to document procurement procedures is in line with the fundamental requirement of the EU-wide principle of transparency.
Only proper and time-relevant documentation on decision-making and justification gives a guarantee of transparency in the procurement process and, above all, guarantees effective legal protection of the interests of each tenderer in cases where the value of the contract exceeds the European limits.
Proper documentation is also an essential part of anti-corruption measures, as the obligation to provide detailed justification for individual decisions and their justification significantly reduces the scope for manipulation.
In cases of European procurement procedures, the contracting authority must notify the completion of these procedures to the EU's Office of Official Publications within 48 days of the date of award of the contract.
The Office publishes the notice in the Official Journal of the EU and this also fulfils the transparency requirement.
The contracting authority must summarise the essential details of its procurement procedures and communicate them to the European Commission through the relevant national ministries.
On this basis, the European Commission produces annual statistics to provide an overview of the development of the single market in the field of construction.
Provisions of German and Czech procurement law
Same or similar provisions
Both countries fully transposed into their national legislation the provisions of the European law on documentation and notification obligations.
The provisions on documentation and notification for European statistics purposes are almost identical in both countries.
Different provisions
In the Czech Republic, the obligation to notify the award of a contract applies not only to European procedures, but also to national procurement procedures.
The notice period is only 15 days from the conclusion of the contract.
In Germany, the obligation to notify procurement procedures, in addition to those which are mandatory under European law, applies only to restricted procedures exceeding the limit of €25K (£625K) and direct procedures exceeding the limit of €15K (£375K) including VAT.
Only interested parties are informed below this limit.
Main control findings
BRH
Documentation
A large number of the terms of reference were not fully documented.
The notification did not contain all the necessary information, e.g. reasons for the supply specifications and national rules.
In one case, the contracting authority did not explain why it had contracted a tenderer whose tender did not include mandatory particulars.
In several cases, some required records were also missing.
Award Publication
In the case of some European procedures, the contracting authority erred in the publication of the notice.
In several of the terms of reference, there was no evidence of publication of the notice.
Notification obligation
The statistically cleared files included the required minimum of information and were created and sent on time.
Preliminary transparency
In most cases, the procuring entity disclosed the contract result.
In the case of one sponsor, contract information was not disclosed due to a communication error.
It took the contracting authority six months to notice the error.
Consequently, the outcome of the proceedings was not disclosed during that period.
OWN
Documentation
For events related to the organisation of a major sporting event, significant shortcomings in the documentation of the tender process were identified, such as incorrect bidding price data in the evaluation committee's meeting protocols.
In addition, some of the documents opening the envelopes were not signed.
Award Publication
In preparation for the repair and maintenance of the roads, the construction of the motorway and the road circuit, the contract award notices were sent after the deadline or were not published.
Summary
Provisions on documentation and notification and notification obligations include European requirements and are almost identical in both countries.
This generally ensures the transparency of procurement procedures and final selection.
The Czech reporting obligations are somewhat stricter compared to the German ones.
The checks revealed shortcomings substantially reducing the value of the dossier as a means of preventing corruption.
THISsegmentISintentionallyLEFTblank
Contracting entities should continue to enhance the transparency of their procurement procedures and fully comply with reporting and notification requirements.
Summary evaluation and recommendations
Parallel audits of the SAO and BRH focused on two areas that are essential for the public procurement process in both the Czech Republic and Germany, namely the application of EU procurement law and the prevention of corruption.
EU legislation provides an opportunity for international competition within the EU to create a single European market.
Measures to prevent corruption are not only intended to avoid it or at least to reduce it, but also to prevent financial damage and possible loss of confidence in the integrity of government.
The audit findings of the two audit institutions - the SAO and BRH - with regard to the application of the two countries' procurement law and the comparison of its respective provisions give an inside view of the common and different elements that may assist the two countries in the further development and application of this law.
Application of EU procurement law
Both countries transposed EU procurement law into their national legislation.
There is thus an important common base in both countries for public procurement and the fulfilment of basic requirements in the area of: competition; equal treatment of candidates; transparency of procurement procedures.
Contracting authorities reinforce these requirements in part with very extensive measures.
The Czech Republic has extended public procurement provisions requiring a European procedure to procedures that are below EU limits and are therefore governed by national legislation.
Nevertheless, checks have shown that contracting authorities are not always fully compliant with all relevant provisions of EU requirements.
German contracting authorities should continue to improve the quality of their procurement procedures in order to eliminate the irregularities mentioned, taking into account the implementation of EU requirements.
In the Czech Republic, the controls identified risks affecting economy and achieving value for money.
These include, in particular, an incomplete definition of the subject-matter of the contract, which resulted in additional works often awarded in breach of the law, a lack of definition of the price envisaged, and the requisite qualifications, which narrowed down the potential pool of tenderers and thus reduced competition, particularly in the field of transport works.
Preventing corruption
The joint inspections were not aimed at detecting cases of corruption.
The reason was that both BRH and the SAO did not have sufficient tools to do so.
They can nevertheless examine whether the competent authorities of both countries are taking measures to curb corruption and manipulation in public procurement and how these measures are applied.
In terms of preventing corruption, the two countries have focused on different aspects.
Above all, German provisions give room for broad and unrestricted competition, transparency, and controls as a central element in preventing corruption.
The Czech regulations are mainly aimed at enhancing the transparency of procurement procedures.
BRH
In Germany, there are general principles for preventing corruption that apply to the entire federal administration.
These principles have also been incorporated into the requirements of public procurement and have been adopted to meet the specific needs of such procurement.
The relevant provisions specify, at times with great precision, the ways of complying with the requirements to prevent corruption during the procurement process and the choice of the people included therein.
In the BRH's view, the principle of preference for open procurement procedures and calls for participation is a particularly appropriate tool to substantially reduce the risk of corruption in public procurement, while limiting competition increases the risk of manipulation.
Risk analysis and adequate controls (in particular the principle of cross-checking), transparency requirements, procurement documents and various organisational measures are also effective mechanisms for preventing corruption and manipulation.
The BRH therefore does not feel the need for substantial changes to the current provisions that public institutions must comply with when awarding public contracts.
Anti-corruption measures are widely applied in practice.
Irregularities found in some areas have highlighted weaknesses in compliance with some requirements.
Therefore, contracting authorities should provide further training to their staff, familiarising them with anti-corruption requirements and further raising their awareness in this area.
A significant proportion of the weaknesses identified relate to activities delegated by the construction administration to private entrepreneurs.
However, the absence of adequate controls in the delegation of powers increases the risk of abuse.
Activities susceptible to manipulation and corruption should therefore only be transferred to third parties if the administration can guarantee a sufficient degree of protection against unauthorised interference.
OWN
In the Czech Republic, anti-corruption strategies have been adopted at government level, affecting the area of public procurement in a significant way.
The audit actions of the SAO took place during the period of implementation of one of the most important parts of this strategy, namely a significant amendment of the Public Procurement Act and the adoption of the subsequent implementing regulations.
The strategy adopted and the new statutory provisions can be effective steps to improve the situation.
Under the new rules, the contracting authority must, for example, justify the effectiveness of the contract, establish the anticipated value of the contract, the adequacy of the requirements for qualifications, establish the basic evaluation criteria and the way tenders are evaluated, and properly document and publish everything.
Recommendations
Overall, both the SAO and BRH have a favourable opinion of the ways in which EU requirements are implemented.
Their broad application in practice has confirmed that EU requirements for competition, equal treatment and transparency form an appropriate basis for public procurement within the EU.
The supranational model, established in the EU, can also serve as a base for similar projects aimed at facilitating and promoting international competition.
Taking into account the great potential for material and non-material damage resulting from manipulation and corruption in connection with public procurement, both BRH and the SAO insist that it is the responsibility of the contracting authorities to continue and improve measures against corruption and manipulation where possible.
In particular, organisational structures and procurement processes should be continuously monitored for weaknesses and risks to identify and respond appropriately to the need for remedial action.
Compared to the practice in Germany, it has emerged that the share of open procurement procedures should be increased in the Czech Republic, giving room for competition and substantially reducing the possibility of corrupt behaviour.
Parcel procurement may also have a positive impact on the price achieved in cases where this is permissible and appropriate for the contracting authority. The higher number of bidders puts pressure on the price achieved.
Ensuring its organisation also has an influence on the conduct of the procurement process.
In Germany, it is provided by special state organisations, while in the Czech Republic, with the exception of transport structures, it is organised by the central authorities themselves or through contracting organisations.
Both the SAO and BRH wish to further develop the knowledge in their work where appropriate and to address it with their parliament and government beyond their joint project.
Moreover, they believe that the principles of procurement law and its implementation, as described in this report, will also be of interest at the level of the International Organisation of Supreme Audit Institutions (INTOSAI), as these principles and findings are of general importance to effectively tackle corruption and to ensure that public contracts are awarded under a regime of well-functioning and fair competition and that value for money is achieved.

JOINT FINAL REPORT on the Results of Parallel Controls on the Administration of Excise Duties in the Slovak Republic and the Czech Republic
Joint final report
May 2017
Supreme Audit Office of the Slovak Republic
Supreme Audit Office of the Czech Republic
Introduction Word
The submitted joint report reports on the progress and outcome of international cooperation in parallel controls carried out by the Supreme Audit Office of the Slovak Republic and the Supreme Audit Office of the Czech Republic, focusing on excise management in 2012-2015.
The cooperation between the supreme audit institutions of the two countries was carried out on the basis of a cooperation agreement between the SAO of the Slovak Republic and the SAO of the Czech Republic in the execution of parallel controls aimed at the management of excise duties, which was concluded in August 2015.
The topic of parallel controls was chosen based on the fact that the two EU Member States are based on common European legislation in order to see how it is applied at national level, how the system of excise management is set up and how the systems in the SR and the CR differ.
The greatest benefits of the control actions carried out were in exchanging experience, identifying differences and identifying good practice.
Last but not least, the benefit of finding that international comparability of data on the performance and efficiency of excise administration is very limited without parallel controls.
Miloslav Kala,
Karol Mitrík,
President
President
Supreme Audit Office of the Czech Republic
Supreme Audit Office of the Slovak Republic
about the results of parallel controls of the SPD administration in SR and CR
Summary and evaluation
The legislation on excise duties in the Czech Republic and the Slovak Republic and their administration is in line with EU legislation and significantly exceeds EU requirements.
Differences have been identified between the adjustment of excise duties in the Slovak Republic and the Czech Republic; in the Czech Republic, the verification of the economic stability of applicants for the issuance of permits and the mandatory marking and colouring of selected mineral oils and certain other oils contribute to the efficient administration of excise duties; In SR, the distribution of control stamps is simpler than in the Czech Republic and less burdensome for tax authorities.
In the fight against evasion of excise duties, supervision of the movement of raw tobacco or tobacco raw material, the implementation of the fuel distributor institute and the registration of sellers of consumer alcohol packaging have proved good practice in both countries.
Revenue from excise duties in the SR and the Czech Republic is growing more slowly than other tax revenues.
The EU's objectives of reducing labour taxation, introducing eco-saving measures and reducing dependence on tobacco and alcohol have not yet been met in the Czech Republic and the Slovak Republic, inter alia because there is no indexation of excise rates in both countries.
This has the effect of reducing the materiality of excise duties in total tax revenues and, as a result of increasing purchasing power, a real reduction in the tax burden of excise duties.
The introduction of environmental measures has long been financially supported by the state, but the measures have a negative impact on excise revenue, which will thus need to be replaced in the future by other revenues or by a reduction in the state's spending.
Administrative expenditure incurred to manage excise duties in both the Czech Republic and the Slovak Republic has been growing over the long term, mainly due to wage increases, and their efficiency has been declining.
In the SR, this efficiency is also negatively affected by expenditure on the computerisation of excise administration (IT expenditure) and expenditure incurred on wages of employees performing permanent tax supervision in alcohol tax warehouses.
The electronisation of excise administration in SR has not had a significant effect on the reduction in the number of tax administrations.
The SAO of the Czech Republic and the SAO of the Slovak Republic recommend that the FŘ SR reconsider the merits of the exercise of permanent tax supervision in tax warehouses on ethyl alcohol.
In the Czech Republic, the efficiency of administrative expenditure was affected by the fact that the Customs Administration of the Czech Republic did not apply procedures during the audited period which would have led to a reduction in the personnel intensity of excise administration.
The number of comparable administrative actions of tax administrations was similar in the Slovak Republic and the Czech Republic in the different years of the audited period, while the number of comparable tax entities in the Slovak Republic is about a third lower than in the Czech Republic.
Tax administrators in the Slovak Republic perform administrative tasks mainly in the form of physical exercise of tax supervision, while tax administrators in the Czech Republic emphasize remote supervision and risk analysis.
The management of excise duties in the Slovak Republic is more administratively demanding than in the Czech Republic and, given the ratio of the number of tax operations to the number of entities, more costly.
The electronisation of excise administration in the Slovak Republic and the Czech Republic took place in different areas.
In the Slovak Republic, the administration of excise tax returns is fully electronised; in the Czech Republic, documents proving the taxation of selected products in free tax circulation are transmitted electronically.
Both methods of electronisation promote the efficient management of excise duties and therefore the SAO of the Czech Republic and the SAO of the Slovak Republic recommend applying them not only in both countries.
The total expenditure for the introduction of the EMCS could not be compared as the PSR only provided expenditure data for the upgrade of the system in the period 2011-2015, but the expenditure was also made before this period.
The expenses incurred in that period only for the upgrade of EMCS were about 20% higher in SR than those incurred in the Czech Republic both for the introduction of EMCS and for the upgrade of this system over more than a decade.
Excise tax managers in the Czech Republic and the Slovak Republic did not achieve comparable values of quality indicators when spending comparably.
The level of surveillance is comparable in both countries, but in the SR, the management of excise duties is significantly more resource-intensive.
The introduction of the Electronic System for Monitoring the Movement of Excise Products (EMCS) has made excise tax administration more efficient, but lower in the SR as significantly higher expenditure was spent on the introduction of the system and its modernisation than in the Czech Republic.
Basic information
The cooperation of the two audit institutions was carried out under the Cooperation Treaty in carrying out parallel controls on the management of excise duties on 18.8.2015.
The SAO of the Czech Republic and the SAO of the Slovak Republic agreed to carry out parallel checks, primarily focused on issues of excise administration, the results of which will allow analysis, mutual comparison and evaluation of the findings.
Joint audit questions were agreed on which the parallel controls were based.
Subject characteristics and objectives of parallel control:
The aim of the parallel controls was to compare the performance of excise managers, taking into account qualitative and quantitative indicators, and to identify weaknesses in the excise management process.
In particular, the following main audit questions were to be answered:
Did excise tax managers in the Czech Republic and the Slovak Republic achieve comparable values of quality indicators when spending comparably?
Has the introduction of the Electronic Excise Movement and Control System (EMCS) made excise administration more efficient and has the expenditure to introduce it been expedient?
Parallel checks should have identified the causes of significant differences affecting the efficiency and effectiveness of the tax administration in the Czech Republic and the Slovak Republic, especially in the regulatory and organisational structure setting.
THISsegmentISintentionallyLEFTblank
The Directorate-General for Customs (DG) and the Financial Directorate of the Slovak Republic (DG SR) have joined the Joint Declaration on Cooperation between the Visegrad Four countries, the objectives of the Declaration include the areas of excise duties including, inter alia, the fight against fraud, exploring the possibility of harmonising legislation and seeking harmonised interpretations on the rules of selected problems, exploring the possibility of joint activities to increase efficiency in detecting fraud and sharing experiences and best practices.
1 The EMCS is operated under Decision No 1152/2003/EC of the European Parliament and of the Council of 16 June 2003 on computerising the movement and surveillance of excisable products.
Identification of the audit carried out by the SAO of the Czech Republic
Check number: 15/33
Subject of control: Administration of excise duties
Objective of the audit: The aim of the audit of the SAO of the Czech Republic was to examine the customs authorities' procedure for the administration of excise duties, in particular for the monitoring of shipments in the system of conditional exemption from excise duties and for securing tax (customs debts); verify that the excise management system is set up to ensure the efficiency of state budget revenue collection.
Period audited: 2012-2015 and, in case of factual context, the period before and after
Controlled Person: Directorate-General of Customs
Identification of the inspection carried out by the SAO SR
Check number: KA-023/2016/1050
Subject of control: Administration of excise duties
Objective of the inspection: The aim of the inspection was an independent and objective examination of whether the FŘ SR and the customs offices (CA) operate economically, efficiently and effectively in accordance with universally binding legislation in the management of excise duties.
Controlled period: years 2012-2015
Controlled Person: Finance Directorate of the Slovak Republic
Legislation on excise duties in the Czech Republic and the Slovak Republic
The excise administration legislation is divided into the EU regulatory level (see Annex 1) and the national regulatory level (see Annex 2).
Council Directive 2008/118/EC concerning the general arrangements for excise duty and repealing Directive 92/12/EEC defines excise duty and lays down the basic rules for the management of taxes and the treatment of energy products and electricity, tobacco products, alcohol and alcoholic beverages.
This directive allows taxes on consumption to be imposed on other products.
In particular, EU legislation lays down minimum conditions for: the level of tax rates for each type of product selected, the production, storage, processing and holding of products subject to tax, chargeability, exemption and refund, supervision of the transport of selected products between EU Member States, international cooperation and the keeping of registers on economic operators and tax warehouses.
EU legislation makes it possible to introduce control procedures and institutes at national level, provided they do not distort competition.
Excise Tax Legislation in the Czech Republic
Definition of legislation in the Czech Republic
In the Czech Republic, the legislation on excise duties is concentrated in the Law on excise duties (Law 353/2003 Coll.), which governs the administration of the mineral oil tax, the alcohol tax, the tobacco product tax, the beer tax, the wine and intermediate tax and the raw tobacco tax.
This law defines the forms of treatment of products subject to excise duties, the taxpayer, the authorisation procedure, the conditions for the transport of products, the declaration of the tax and the penalties for infringements.
In addition, the law and subsequent decrees regulate certain other conditions for the handling of mineral oils (mineral oil labelling), restrictions on the sale of spirits and tobacco products, the monitoring of the handling of special mineral oils and the storage of raw tobacco for a purpose other than the manufacture of tobacco products.
Taxes on other energy products (i.e. natural gas and certain other gases, solid fuels and electricity) are regulated in Parts 45 to 47 of Act 261/2007 Coll., on the Stabilisation of Public Budgets.
In the Czech Republic, a number of provisions related to the management of excise duties are contained in special legislation. The Fuel Act No 311/2006 Coll. and the Implementing Regulation govern the conditions for the sale, supply, distribution of fuel and the keeping of a register of fuel entered into free circulation.
Act No 61/1997 Coll., on ethyl alcohol, and its implementing regulations define the conditions for the production, storage, registration and control of the production and circulation of ethyl alcohol.
Act No 307/2013 Coll., on the compulsory marking of ethyl alcohol, and the relevant implementing provisions lay down the compulsory marking of ethyl alcohol in consumer packaging, the conditions for handling surveillance tapes and the conditions for handling ethyl alcohol (i.e. for the production, processing, sale or other transfer, transport, storage, taking over, use, possession, import or export of ethyl alcohol).
Tax procedure procedures not provided for in the special rules are governed by Act No 280/2009 Coll., the Tax Code.
The penalties are imposed in accordance with the procedures laid down in Act No 500/2004 Coll., the Code of Administrative Procedure.
The conditions for issuing a trade licence (concession) to operate a business ‘production and processing of fuels and lubricants and distribution of fuel’ and ‘production, treatment and sale of fermentation spirit, drinking spirit and spirits’ are governed by Act No 455/1991 Coll., on the Trade Licensing Business (the Trade Licensing Act).
The Czech Republic has used the authorisation to establish control procedures and institutes to control the transport of selected products in free tax circulation and to monitor compliance with the legislation when handling selected products for which excise duty has not been paid as well as when handling selected products for which duty has already been paid.
These are the monitoring of the handling of special mineral oils and raw tobacco, the labelling of certain other mineral oils, the labelling of tobacco products and ethyl alcohol in consumer packaging.
The Czech Republic made use of the authorisation to apply excise duty to other products and introduced excise duty on raw tobacco as one of the measures against tax fraud.
Structure of amendments to legislation for individual types of excise duties in relation to subject of control in the Czech Republic
Following the resolution of the Government of the Czech Republic of 3 October 2012 No 735, to implement the "Zero Tolerance Plan for the Black Market in Spirits," major legislative adjustments took place in the Czech Republic in 2012-2015, introducing a number of measures against tax fraud on excise duty on spirit.
During the same period, there were also major changes in the legislation for the mineral oil tax, the tobacco products tax and the criminality of preparing tax cuts.
Criminal law protection of tax revenue
With effect from 1 July 2016, the criminality of preparing the offence of "reduction of tax, fee and similar compulsory payment" is introduced. The arrangements made should allow police authorities to take more effective and timely measures to reduce tax fraud, e.g. by deploying operational search resources.
General provisions for excise management
The issue of a licence to dispose of excisable products under the Excise Duties Act, with effect from 1.01.2015, is subject to: reliability; debt-free; economic stability; the right to operate a business enabling the exercise of an authorisation-related activity; a situation in which the claimant is not in liquidation or bankruptcy.
In particular, the aim of the legislation was to address the issue of the build-up of tax arrears by reducing the entities authorised to deal with products subject to excise duty.
The condition of economic stability newly allows the tax administration to take into account the financial situation of tax subjects both in the context of the authorisation procedure and after the authorisation has been granted.
The purpose of this measure is to prevent entities that do not have sufficient financial revenue to comply properly with tax obligations from handling the selected products.
To assess the economic stability of the proposer, indicators (profitability, liquidity, financial stability, activity, labour productivity) have been established on the basis of which the financial analysis is carried out.
In addition to these indicators, the administrative discretion of the tax authorities depends on the individual circumstances of the procedure.
The law provides for a periodicity of verification of compliance with the economic stability condition due to the timeliness of the data — the verification is carried out once a year following the deadline for filing income tax returns.
The tax authorities have the option to lay down other conditions necessary for the administration of the tax.
If the permit holder does not comply with the conditions, the tax administration is entitled to revoke the permit, or to revoke it deferred if necessary.
Of the 2,609 entities for which the Customs Administration of the Czech Republic examined the condition of economic stability, 18.6% were found to be economically unstable with a high risk of arrears if the tax liability exceeded the amount of collateral tax (e.g. recurring beneficiary).
Permits were revoked for 10 entities, other applicants increased the security of the tax, capitalised their contribution to the company or substantiated up-to-date accounting data showing that they already met all the conditions for issuing a permit.
In addition to the claimant, the conditions of reliability and indebtedness must be met by a natural person who is or is a member of the statutory body or by a natural person who, although not a member of the claimant's statutory body, carries out that activity (a chain of legal persons in the capacity of the statutory body or its member, so-called tied entities).
By the end of 2016, more than 30,000 linked subjects had been screened.
The registers kept under the Excise Duties Act (Sections 37 to 40 and Section 60 of Act No 353/2003 Coll.) contain data on:
the type, name, code and quantity of goods produced, received, delivered or dispatched, broken down by type of tax treatment arrangement (conditional exemption arrangement, importation, re-introduction to a conditional exemption arrangement, exportation, free circulation, captive consumption, loss);
the identifier (SEED ID) of the recipient or supplier of the selected products, the declarant, the date of the event and the record entry;
transport of selected products (e.g. means of transport, carriers, transport numbers);
the registration numbers of the related documents;
notification of the release for free tax circulation of the spirit in a consumer pack marked with a surveillance tape and details of its distribution.
A record of the take-over after transport shall be made as soon as it is completed.
In the case of an electronically maintained register, the register must be kept or allow output in the format xls, xlsx, ods.
Alcohol
After 2012, the Czech Republic introduced a complex of measures designed to provide a detailed overview of the activities of the entities (see Annex 3, Schedule A) from the production of raw alcohol to the marking with surveillance tape to its distribution to the final seller or to the entitled user of the exempt alcohol.
In the Czech Republic, the production facility for the production of ethyl alcohol, which is not in operation, is subject to supervision by customs authorities.
The aim of the new legislation was to increase the overview over the processing of spirit drinks and the production of spirit drinks and to identify those handling spirit in consumer packaging, to reduce the disposal of spirit to financially stable and reliable persons and to ensure constant supervision of the production and marking of spirit drinks.
The most significant measures are: the production, intake, stock records and the release of ethyl alcohol contain information on the quantity of ethyl alcohol by stages of production and processing of ethyl alcohol, indicating the numbers of the documents.
Limitation of the volume of the consumer packaging, the material of the packaging and limitation of the number of packages with broken surveillance tape at the point of final sale or issue to the consumer.
Consumer packaging means a container or other packaging with a capacity of not more than one litre or three litres if it is a container made of glass.
Spirits in larger packs are regarded as non-branded and prohibited from being handled.
At the point of final sale to a natural person for his or her personal consumption, a maximum of one consumer pack with a broken surveillance tape, or three if fitted with a one-way valve, can be found.
Compulsory registration of a person who places ethyl alcohol in consumer packaging products having at least 15% alcohol by volume into free tax circulation and is: an importer of ethyl alcohol, a producer of ethyl alcohol in the Czech Republic or a tax warehouse operator under a conditional exemption regime, provided that personal and technical conditions are met.
The personal conditions — reliability, integrity and non-disclosure of the disqualification — must be met by the applicant and a person in a position of authority or responsible representative.
The applicant must provide a bail of between CZK 100,000 and CZK 5,000,000 (i.e. about €3,700 and €185,000), depending on the estimated amount of control tapes collected.
The technical conditions for registration are a suitable place to mark ethyl alcohol and to equip the place of marking or tax warehouse with a continuous-traffic camera tracking device and remote access by the tax authorities.
Registration markings of the surveillance tape are in place for the marking of spirit drinks of more than 0,06 litre.
When the control tapes are transported, the registration mark of the control tapes must appear on the transport documents or customs declaration at export.
The check tape until 30.11.2013 contained a 10-digit registration code, which included a four-digit registration sampling number, an application code for the allocation of check tapes and a classification code according to the ethanol concentration level by volume.
The surveillance tape from 1.12.2013 newly contains two types of identifiers and the amount of ethyl alcohol in the consumer package expressed in millilitres of ethanol at 20 °C, rounded down to the whole millilitres.
The register of surveillance tapes for the marking of spirit drinks contains daily records of each method of handling the surveillance tapes according to the registration codes, as well as the documents on the basis of which the registration took place.
When specific surveillance tapes are destroyed, lost or stolen, the holder shall, on the following working day following the operative event, electronically notify the tax authorities of the registration code of the control tapes concerned and their number.
Introducing new offences and adjusting the level of penalties imposed for infringements in the handling of surveillance tapes.
Penalties may be imposed up to the level of excise duty or up to ten times that amount (e.g. in case of destruction or loss of control tapes in excess of 0.1% of the total quantity of individual control tape orders placed, in case of failure to return the control tape within the statutory time limit).
Restrictions on the treatment of surveillance tapes only to registered keepers who are not allowed to pass them on to another person, with the exception of their provision to a foreign supplier for the purpose of marking consumer-packaged spirits produced outside the Czech Republic.
Exported surveillance tapes must be placed on the consumer packaging and imported back no later than nine months after receipt from the tax authorities.
Compulsory registration of a distributor of ethyl alcohol in a consumer pack, provided that similar personal conditions are fulfilled as for the person required to mark ethyl alcohol and with an obligation to post bail of up to CZK 5,000,000 (i.e. approx. €185,000).
Definition of persons for the sale or purchase of ethyl alcohol in consumer packaging.
Beneficiaries are obliged to sell their consumer-packaged ethyl alcohol to other registered persons or to final sellers, or they can sell it as a final seller.
They may acquire ethyl alcohol from other registered persons, an insolvency practitioner, an administrative body, organisational units of the State or non-registered persons in the execution of a tax execution.
Notices of the placing of ethyl alcohol or sales in consumer packing for free tax circulation are required to be sent by the holders and distributors of ethyl alcohol immediately following the operative event to the tax authorities by means of an information system maintained by the tax administration (ORO web client) or in accordance with content in xml format.
The notification shall include the number of control tapes used, the volume and type of consumer packaging, the percentage by volume of ethyl alcohol, the European Commodity Code (EAN), the identification of the distributor or final seller, as well as the name of the product and the batch of production.
Tax entities sent around 10.5 million notifications in 2014 and 2015.
Compulsory concession (a specific form of business licence) not only for the production and conditioning of ethyl alcohol, but also for the sale of ethyl alcohol in consumer packaging by an entrepreneur to a natural person for his personal use or to another legal or business-like natural person for his own internal use.
Licensed trades can only be carried out after a decision has been taken by the Trade Licensing Office upon the fulfilment of professional competence and other conditions.
No prescribed professional competence is required for the sale of ethyl alcohol.
Benefits for tax administrations include a review of sellers and their establishments and restrictions
trading on reliable entities.
In the Czech Republic, around 94,000 concessions were registered in 2014 and in 2015 around 104,000 concessions for a field including production, preparation of fermentation spirit, drinking spirit, spirits and other alcoholic beverages, and sales.
Checks on compliance with the legislation on the treatment of products subject to excise duties in the Czech Republic are also ensured in their activities outside the tax administration: trade offices, the State Agricultural and Food Inspection Authority, the Financial Administration of the Czech Republic, the Czech Trade Inspection Authority.
If it finds that there has been a breach of duty or prohibition, it shall immediately inform the excise duty officer.
Mineral oils
The Czech Republic has put in place a comprehensive system of supervision (see Annex 3, Schedule B) to which mineral oils subject to excise duty but also selected mineral oils subject to excise duty unencumbered which have been abused for tax evasion.
The system includes supervision from the production of mineral oil through the storage, transport, marking and colouring of selected mineral oils, the marking of certain other mineral oils to delivery to the final consumer or user and, in some cases, the consumption of mineral oils.
Customs supervision includes the production of markings for the marking and handling of mineral oils.
Mineral oils listed under Combined Nomenclature codes 2710 19 25 (kerosene others), 2710 19 29 (other medium oils not elsewhere specified) and heavy gas oils 2710 19 41, 2710 19 45 and 2710 19 49 are subject to the marking and dyeing of selected mineral oils unless their marking is prohibited.
For example, benzole, toluene, xylol and lubricating oils and other oils intended for purposes other than engine propulsion are subject to the labelling of certain other mineral oils.
Selected mineral oils and certain other mineral oils can only be branded and coloured with a tracer if authorised by the tax authorities.
THISsegmentISintentionallyLEFTblank
THISsegmentISintentionallyLEFTblank
The most significant new measures since 2012:
